The Principle of Sulh on Debts: Isqat, Not Mu'awada
States the legal maxim for interpreting settlements in debt contracts and applies it to various scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for interpreting settlements on debts and apply it to distinguish valid from invalid settlements.

Core Idea
Sulh on a debt is remission, not a sale—avoiding riba by treating part of the claim as dropped.

When a creditor settles a debt for less than the full amount, is this a new sale subject to all the rules of exchange? The Hanafi school says no: such a settlement is legally constructed as the creditor receiving part of his right and voluntarily remitting the remainder. This construction, based on the principle of taharri al-tashih (validating transactions where possible), avoids the riba that would inevitably arise if the settlement were treated as a sale. In this lesson, we will unpack the principle and apply it to a series of practical scenarios from al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
State the General Principle
Identify the core legal maxim: any settlement (sulh) on a debt owed by virtue of a debt contract (ʿaqd al-mudāyanah) is not to be interpreted as a new exchange (muʿāwaḍah) but rather as the creditor taking part of his right and remitting the remainder.
This is the foundational rule; remember the key terms: isqat (remission) vs. mu'awada (exchange).
2
Understand the Rationale
Examine why the law adopts this construction: because treating it as a sale would invariably lead to riba (usury) due to differences in quantity, quality, or time. The rational transaction principle (taharri al-tashih) commands us to validate a transaction if possible.
The alternative (mu'awada) would invalidate the settlement in most cases, so isqat is the only workable construction.
3
Apply the Principle to Simple Cases
Work through the first two examples: a debt of 1000 dirhams settled for 500 dirhams is valid—it is isqat of 500. Similarly, a debt of 1000 good dirhams settled for 500 bad dirhams is valid—it is isqat of quantity and quality.
In both cases, the creditor receives something less than the full right, so remission is evident.
4
Analyze Cases Involving Time and Different Species
Consider cases where the settlement involves deferment, spot payment, or a different currency. For example, settling a deferred debt for the same amount on a new term is valid deferment; but settling for dinars on credit is invalid because dinars are not part of the original debt and the exchange would be ribawi.
The key is whether the settlement can be construed as a modification of the original right (deferment, remission) or must be a new exchange.
5
Identify Exceptions and Special Rules
Note when the treatment shifts from isqat to mu'awada: if the settlement is for exactly the same thing (mithl) or better quality (ajwad), it is treated as an exchange of like for like, requiring immediate possession (qabd) in the session to avoid riba.
This exception preserves the principle while handling the edge case where no remission occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ وَقَعَ عَلَيْهِ الصُّلْحُ وَهُوَ مُسْتَحَقٌّ بِعَقْدِ الْمُدَايَنَةِ لَمْ يُحْمَلْ عَلَى الْمُعَاوَضَةِ وَإِنَّمَا يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنَّهُ اسْتَوْفَى بَعْضَ حَقِّهِ وَأَسْقَطَ بَاقِيَهُ. كَمَنْ لَهُ عَلَى آخَرَ أَلْفُ دِرْهَمٍ فَصَالَحَهُ عَلَى خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ وَكَمَنْ لَهُ عَلَى آخَرَ أَلْفُ جِيَادٍ فَصَالَحَهُ عَلَى خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ زُيُوفٍ جَازَ فَكَأَنَّهُ أَبْرَاهُ عَنْ بَعْضِ حَقِّهِ، وَهَذَا لِأَنَّ تَصَرُّفَ الْعَاقِلِ يُتَحَرَّى تَصْحِيحُهُ مَا أَمْكَنَ وَلَا وَجْهَ لِتَصْحِيحِهِ مُعَاوَضَةً لِإِفْضَائِهِ إِلَى الرِّبَا، فَجُعِلَ إِسْقَاطًا لِلْبَعْضِ فِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ الْأُولَى وَلِلْبَعْضِ وَالصِّفَةِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ. وَلَوْ صَالَحَ عَلَى أَلْفٍ مُؤَجَّلَةٍ جَازَ وَكَأَنَّهُ أَجَّلَ نَفْسَ الْحَقِّ، لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ جَعْلُهُ مُعَاوَضَةً لِأَنَّ بَيْعَ الدَّرَاهِمِ بِمِثْلِهَا نَسِيئَةً لَا يَجُوزُ، فَحَمَلْنَاهُ عَلَى التَّأْخِيرِ. وَلَوْ صَالَحَهُ عَلَى دَنَانِيرَ إِلَى شَهْرٍ لَمْ يَجُزْ لِأَنَّ الدَّنَانِيرَ غَيْرُ مُسْتَحَقَّةٍ بِعَقْدِ الْمُدَايَنَةِ فَلَا يُمْكِنُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى التَّأْخِيرِ، وَلَا وَجْهَ لَهُ سِوَى الْمُعَاوَضَةِ، وَبَيْعُ الدَّرَاهِمِ بِالدَّنَانِيرِ نَسِيئَةً لَا يَجُوزُ فَلَمْ يَصِحَّ الصُّلْحُ. وَقَالَ: وَلَوْ كَانَتْ لَهُ أَلْفٌ مُؤَجَّلَةً فَصَالَحَهُ عَلَى خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ حَالَّةٍ لَمْ يَجُزْ لِأَنَّ الْمُعَجَّلَ خَيْرٌ مِنَ الْمُؤَجَّلِ وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مُسْتَحَقٍّ بِالْعَقْدِ، فَيَكُونُ بِإِزَاءِ مَا حَطَّهُ عَنْهُ وَذَلِكَ اعْتِيَاضٌ عَنِ الْأَجَلِ وَهُوَ حَرَامٌ. وَإِنْ كَانَ لَهُ أَلْفُ سُودٍ فَصَالَحَهُ عَلَى خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ بِيضٍ لَمْ يَجُزْ لِأَنَّ الْبِيضَ غَيْرُ مُسْتَحَقَّةٍ بِعَقْدِ الْمُدَايَنَةِ وَهِيَ زَائِدَةٌ وَصْفًا، فَيَكُونُ مُعَاوَضَةَ الْأَلْفِ بِخَمْسِ مِائَةٍ وَزِيَادَةِ وَصْفٍ وَهُوَ رِبًا، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا صَالَحَ عَنِ الْأَلْفِ الْبِيضِ عَلَى خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ سُودٍ لِأَنَّهُ إِسْقَاطُ بَعْضِ حَقِّهِ قَدْرًا وَوَصْفًا، وَبِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا صَالَحَ عَلَى قَدْرِ الدَّيْنِ وَهُوَ أَجْوَدُ لِأَنَّهُ مُعَاوَضَةُ الْمِثْلِ بِالْمِثْلِ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ بِالصِّفَةِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ الْقَبْضُ فِي الْمَجْلِسِ. وَلَوْ كَانَ عَلَيْهِ أَلْفُ دِرْهَمٍ وَمِائَةُ دِينَارٍ فَصَالَحَ عَلَى مِائَةِ دِرْهَمٍ حَالَّةٍ أَوْ إِلَى شَهْرٍ صَحَّ الصُّلْحُ لِأَنَّهُ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَجْعَلَ إِسْقَاطًا لِلدَّنَانِيرِ كُلِّهَا وَالدَّرَاهِمِ إِلَّا مِائَةً وَتَأْجِيلًا لِلْبَاقِي فَلَا يُجْعَلُ مُعَاوَضَةً تَصْحِيحًا لِلْعَقْدِ وَلِأَنَّ مَعْنَى الْإِسْقَاطِ فِيهِ أَلْزَمُ.
He said: Everything upon which a settlement is made while it is owed due to a debt contract is not carried on mutual exchange but is carried on that he received part of his right and dropped the rest. Like one who has a thousand dirhams due from another and settles for five hundred, and like one who has a thousand good dirhams due from another and settles for five hundred bad dirhams — it is valid, as if he acquitted him from part of his right. This is because the transaction of a rational person is sought to be validated as much as possible, and there is no way to validate it as an exchange because it leads to usury, so it is made a remission of part in the first case, and of part and quality in the second. And if he settled for a thousand deferred, it is valid, as if he deferred the right itself, because it is not possible to make it an exchange since selling dirhams for their like on credit is not permitted, so we carried it on deferment. And if he settled for dinars payable after a month, it is not valid because dinars are not owed due to the debt contract, so it cannot be carried on deferment, and there is no face for it except exchange, and selling dirhams for dinars on credit is not permitted, so the settlement is not valid. And he said: And if he had a thousand deferred and he settled for five hundred spot, it is not valid because the spot is better than the deferred and it is not owed by the contract, so it is in exchange for what he reduced from him and that is compensation for deferment, which is forbidden. And if he had a thousand black dirhams and he settled for five hundred white dirhams, it is not valid because the white are not owed by the debt contract and they are superior in quality, so it becomes an exchange of a thousand for five hundred plus an increase in quality, which is usury, contrary to when he settles for the thousand white on five hundred black, because it is a remission of part of his right in quantity and quality, and contrary to when he settles for the amount of the debt while it is better, because it is an exchange of like for like and quality is not considered, except that receipt in the session is required. And if he owed a thousand dirhams and a hundred dinars and he settled for a hundred dirhams spot or to a month, the settlement is valid because it is possible to make it a remission of all the dinars and all the dirhams except a hundred, and a deferment of the rest, so it is not made an exchange to validate the contract, and because the meaning of remission is more fitting in it.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a legal maxim (qāʿidah) from the Hanafi school regarding settlements (ṣulḥ) made on debts arising from a contract of loan or credit sale (ʿaqd al-mudāyanah). The author, al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, states the fundamental rule: such a settlement is not to be interpreted as a new sale or exchange (muʿāwaḍah). Instead, it is legally considered as the claimant taking part of his right and voluntarily remitting the remainder. This construction is necessary to avoid ribā (usury), which would arise if the settlement were treated as an exchange involving disparity in quantity, quality, or deferment. The text then provides several applications illustrating the rule. If a creditor has 1000 dirhams and settles for 500, it is valid — the 500 are taken as part payment and the 500 are remitted. Similarly, if the debt is 1000 good (jīyād) dirhams and settlement is on 500 bad (zuyūf), the settlement is valid; the creditor remits part of the quantity and the superior quality. If the debt is deferred and the settlement is on the same amount but also deferred, it is valid as a deferment of the original debt, not a sale. If the settlement is on dinars (a different currency) for a future date, it is invalid because the dinars are not part of the original debt and the transaction would be an exchange of dirhams for dinars on credit, which is prohibited ribā. If the debt is deferred and settled for a smaller spot amount, it is invalid because the spot payment is in exchange for the reduction of the debt, which is compensation for time (ribā). If the debt is 1000 black (low quality) dirhams and settlement is for 500 white (better quality), it is invalid because the white are not owed and represent an excess in quality, amounting to ribā. However, if the debt is 1000 white and settlement is for 500 black, it is valid because the creditor remits part of the quantity and quality. If the settlement is for the same quantity but of better quality, it is a valid exchange of like for like, but immediate possession in the session is required. Finally, if the debt consists of multiple items (e.g., 1000 dirhams and 100 dinars) and settlement is on 100 dirhams spot or deferred, it is valid because it can be interpreted as remission of all debts except 100 dirhams, and deferment of that 100, avoiding any muʿāwaḍah. The overriding principle is to validate a rational person's transaction wherever possible, and to avoid ribā by construing the settlement as isqāṭ (remission) rather than sale.

Key Terms
الصُّلْح (al-ṣulḥ): Settlement or compromise between disputing parties; in this context, a creditor settling with a debtor for less than the full debt.
عَقْدُ الْمُدَايَنَةِ (ʿaqd al-mudāyanah): Contract of debt, such as a credit sale (bayʿ bi al-nasīʾah) or a loan (qarḍ).
الْمُعَاوَضَة (al-muʿāwaḍah): Mutual exchange; a contract where each party gives something in exchange for something else, like a sale.
الْإِسْقَاط (al-isqāṭ): Remission or dropping of a right; the creditor voluntarily gives up part of his claim.
الرِّبَا (al-ribā): Usury or interest; any excess in exchange of same commodities without immediate possession or equality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle stated in this passage, how is a settlement (sulh) on a debt obligation (mustahaqq bi 'aqd al-mudayanah) legally construed?
A) As a new sale (mu'awada) that must satisfy all conditions of sale.
B) As the creditor receiving part of his right and remitting the remainder (isqat).
C) As a donation (hibah) with no legal effect on the debt.
D) As a cancellation (ibra') of the entire debt with no payment.

Key Takeaway
Sulh on debts from mudayanah is legally isqat, not mu'awada, to avoid riba; only when settling for exactly the same or better does it become a mu'awada requiring immediate possession.

Conditional Ibra' in Sulh: The Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional ibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on conditional ibra', explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A conditional ibra' lapses if the condition fails—Abu Hanifa's view prevails over Abu Yusuf's.

When a creditor says, 'Pay me 500 dirhams tomorrow and you are free from the remaining 500,' does that release become absolute if the debtor pays? What if the debtor fails to pay? The Hanafi masters disagree. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core legal question
Read the passage carefully. The scenario: a debt of 1000 dirhams; the creditor says: 'If you pay 500 dirhams tomorrow, you are free from the rest.' The debtor pays on time. The key question: if the debtor fails to pay, does the full debt revive?
2
Extract the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Find their ruling: the ibra' is conditional, so if the condition fails, the full debt is revived. Note their key arguments: the word 'ala (upon) can indicate a condition; the debtor's promise to pay is a valid motive (gharad) like avoiding insolvency or trade profit; ibra' can be conditioned like hawala.
3
Extract the position of Abu Yusuf
Find his ruling: the ibra' is absolute and does not revert. His argument: the payment is mentioned as if it were consideration (iwad), but since the payment is already obligatory, the mention is void; thus the ibra' becomes unconditional, like starting with ibra' without any condition.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond: the word 'ala is ambiguous; it can mean condition as well as consideration. Since interpreting it as consideration is impossible (because the payment is already due), we interpret it as a condition to validate the transaction. Also, ibra' can be conditional.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the relied-upon view
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the debtor fails to pay, Abu Hanifa/Muhammad allow the creditor to demand the full 1000; Abu Yusuf says the creditor has no claim. The text indicates the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک کے دوسرے پر ہزار درہم ہیں اس نے کہا کہ کل کے دن پانچ سو دیدے تو باقی سے بری ہے، اس نے ایسا ہی کیا تو وہ بری ہو جائے گا
قَالَ وَمَنْ لَهُ عَلى آخرَ الْفُ دِرْهَم فَقَالَ اَذِ إِلَيَّ غَدًا مِنْهَا خَمْسَ مِائَةٍ عَلى أنك برئى مِنَ الْفَضْلِ فَهُوَ بَرِئيٌّ فَإِنْ لَمْ يَدْفَع إِلَيْهِ خَمْسَ مِائَةٍ غَدًا عَادَ عَلَيْهِ الْأَلْفُ وَهُوَ قَوْلُ ابِي حَنِيفَةً وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ لَا يَعُودُ عَلَيْهِ لأَنَّهُ إِبْرَاءُ مُطْلَقَ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ جَعَلَ اَدَاءَ خَمْسَ مِائَةٍ عِوَضًا حَيْثُ ذَكَرَهُ بِكَلِمَةِ عَلَى وَهِيَ لِلْمُعَاوَضَةِ وَالْاَدَاءُ لَا يَصْلُحُ عِوَضًا لِكَوْنِهِ مُسْتَحِقًا عَلَيْهِ فَجَرَى وُجُودُهُ مَجْرَى عَدَمِهِ فَبَقِيَ الْإِبْرَاءُ مُطْلَقًا فَلَا يَعُودُ كَمَا إِذَا بَدَأ بِالْإِبْرَاءِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ هَذَا إِبْرَاء مُقَيَّدٌ بِالشَّرْطِ فَيَفُوتُ بِفَوَاتِهِ لِأَنَّهُ بَدَأَ بِأَدَاءِ خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ فِي الْعَدِ وَانَّهُ يَصْلُحُ غَرْضًا حِذار اِفلَاسِهِ أَوْ تَوَسُّلًا إِلَى تِجَارَةٍ أَرْبَحَ مِنْهُ وَكَلِمَةُ عَلَى إِنْ كَانَتْ لِلْمُعَاوَضَةِ فَهِيَ مُحْتَمَلَةٌ لِلشَّرْطِ لِوُجُودِ مَعْنَى الْمُقَابَلَةِ فِيهِ فَيُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَ تَعَدُّرِ الْحَمْلِ عَلَى الْمُعَاوَضَةِ تَصْحِيحًا لِتَصَرُّفِهِ أَوْ لِأَنَّهُ مُتَعَارَتْ وَالْإِبْرَاءُ مِمَّا يَتَقَيَّدُ بالشَّرْطِ وَإِنْ كَانَ لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ به
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a conditional ibra' (release) in a debt settlement. The scenario: someone owes 1,000 dirhams and says, 'If you pay 500 dirhams tomorrow, you are free from the rest.' The debtor pays on time. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that the debtor is indeed free. However, if the debtor fails to pay the 500 tomorrow, the full 1,000 is revived. Abu Yusuf disagrees: he argues that the ibra' is absolute and does not revert, because the mention of payment is like a consideration (iwad) but since payment is already obligatory, it cannot serve as a valid consideration; thus the ibra' stands unconditional. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond: this is a conditional ibra' (ibra' muqayyad bi al-shart), so it lapses if the condition fails. They interpret the word 'ala (upon) as indicating a condition rather than a consideration, to validate the transaction. The principle: a rational person's statement should be interpreted as validly as possible, and ibra' can be conditioned like hawala (transfer of debt).

Key Terms
إِبْرَاء (ibra'): Release or discharge from a debt; the creditor relinquishes the right to demand payment.
شَرْط (shart): Condition; in ibra', a condition that the debtor perform an act (e.g., pay part) for the release to be effective.
عِوَض (iwad): Consideration or compensation; here, the payment of 500 dirhams is considered as a potential 'iwad for the release.
مُطْلَق (mutlaq): Absolute or unconditional; Abu Yusuf views the ibra' as unconditional despite the mention of payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a debtor fails to pay the 500 dirhams on the agreed date in the conditional ibra' scenario, what happens?
A) The ibra' remains valid and the debtor is still free from the remaining debt
B) The full debt of 1,000 dirhams is revived
C) The debtor must pay only 500 dirhams
D) The creditor must accept 500 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a conditional ibra' (e.g., 'pay half and you are free') is valid; if the condition fails, the debt revives in full.

Conditional Discharge (Ibra') in Sulh: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether conditional release from debt is absolute or conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional ibra' and apply the mufta bihi position to determine the effect of a failed condition.

Core Idea
A conditional discharge fails when the condition fails – Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view is relied upon.

Can a creditor make a discharge (ibra') from debt conditional on receiving part payment? And if the debtor fails to pay on time, does the original debt revive? This lesson examines a famous intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side, and Abu Yusuf on the other. The answer hinges on the interpretation of the word 'ala' (upon/on condition) and whether ibra' can be restricted by a condition. Let's follow the arguments and see which position is mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario and the agreed point
The debtor owes 1000 dirhams. The creditor says: 'Give me 500 tomorrow, and you are free from the remainder.' All agree that if the debtor pays on time, the discharge is effective and the debtor owes nothing. The dispute arises when the debtor fails to pay on time.
The agreed point helps isolate the khilaf.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two') hold that the discharge is conditional; if payment is not made on time, the condition fails, and the original debt of 1000 dirhams returns. Abu Yusuf holds that the discharge is absolute; whether payment is made or not, the debtor is released from the entire debt.
Know who holds what: Tarafayn vs Abu Yusuf.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's argument (the opponent)
Abu Yusuf argues that the word 'ala' (on) indicates exchange (mu'awada). The creditor made the payment a compensation for the discharge. But payment is already obligatory, so it cannot be a valid compensation; thus the discharge stands absolute, as if no condition was mentioned. He analogizes to a case where the discharge is mentioned first (consensus that it's absolute).
His key point: the payment is invalid as compensation, so the condition is ignored.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
They argue that the discharge is explicitly restricted by a condition. The word 'ala' can bear the meaning of condition (shart) because both exchange and condition involve a correspondence ('muqabala'). Since interpreting it as exchange leads to an invalid transaction (the payment cannot be compensation), it must be interpreted as condition to validate the act. Moreover, custom ('urf) treats it as a condition. They also draw an analogy with hawala (transfer of debt) where acquittal is conditional on soundness.
The principle: when one meaning leads to validity and another to invalidity, prefer the valid meaning.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical difference: If debtor fails to pay, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad allow the creditor to claim the full 1000; Abu Yusuf says the creditor cannot claim anything. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two, as it protects the creditor's right and aligns with the parties' intent.
Tarjih is by the later Hanafi masters and the text's commentary.

Source Text

ایک کے دوسرے پر ہزار درہم ہیں اس نے کہا کہ کل کے دن پانچ سو دیدے تو باقی سے بری ہے، اس نے ایسا ہی کیا تو وہ بری ہو جائے گا
قَالَ وَمَنْ لَهُ عَلى آخرَ الْفُ دِرْهَم فَقَالَ اَذِ إِلَيَّ غَدًا مِنْهَا خَمْسَ مِائَةٍ عَلى أنك برئى مِنَ الْفَضْلِ فَهُوَ بَرِئيٌّ فَإِنْ لَمْ يَدْفَع إِلَيْهِ خَمْسَ مِائَةٍ غَدًا عَادَ عَلَيْهِ الْأَلْفُ وَهُوَ قَوْلُ ابِي حَنِيفَةً وَمُحَمَّدٍ وَقَالَ أَبُويُوسُفَ لَا يَعُودُ عَلَيْهِ لأَنَّهُ إِبْرَاءُ مُطْلَقَ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ جَعَلَ اَدَاءَ خَمْسَ مِائَةٍ عِوَضًا حَيْثُ ذَكَرَهُ بِكَلِمَةِ عَلَى وَهِيَ لِلْمُعَاوَضَةِ وَالْاَدَاءُ لَا يَصْلُحُ عِوَضًا لِكَوْنِهِ مُسْتَحِقًا عَلَيْهِ فَجَرَى وُجُودُهُ مَجْرَى عَدَمِهِ فَبَقِيَ الْإِبْرَاءُ مُطْلَقًا فَلَا يَعُودُ كَمَا إِذَا بَدَأ بِالْإِبْرَاءِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ هَذَا إِبْرَاء مُقَيَّدٌ بِالشَّرْطِ فَيَفُوتُ بِفَوَاتِهِ لِأَنَّهُ بَدَأَ بِأَدَاءِ خَمْسِ مِائَةٍ فِي الْعَدِ وَانَّهُ يَصْلُحُ غَرْضًا حِذار اِفلَاسِهِ أَوْ تَوَسُّلًا إِلَى تِجَارَةٍ أَرْبَحَ مِنْهُ وَكَلِمَةُ عَلَى إِنْ كَانَتْ لِلْمُعَاوَضَةِ فَهِيَ مُحْتَمَلَةٌ لِلشَّرْطِ لِوُجُودِ مَعْنَى الْمُقَابَلَةِ فِيهِ فَيُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ عِنْدَ تَعَدُّرِ الْحَمْلِ عَلَى الْمُعَاوَضَةِ تَصْحِيحًا لِتَصَرُّفِهِ أَوْ لِأَنَّهُ مُتَعَارَتْ وَالْإِبْرَاءُ مِمَّا يَتَقَيَّدُ بالشَّرْطِ وَإِنْ كَانَ لَا يَتَعَلَّقُ به كَالْحَوَالَةِ وَسَتَخْرُجُ الْبَدَاءَةِ بِالْإِبْرَاءِ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى
ترجمہ کسی کے دوسرے پر ہزار درہم ہیں۔ اس نے کہا کہ مجھے کل کے دن ان میں سے پانچ سو دے دے اس شرط پر کہ تو باقی سے بری ہے۔ اس نے ایسا ہی کیا تو وہ بری ہو جائے گا اور اگر اس نے کل کے دن پانچ سو درہم نہ دیئے تو اس پر ہزار درہم لوٹ آئیں گے۔ یہی طرفین کا قول ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ نہیں لوٹیں گے۔ کیونکہ یہ ابر مطلق ہے کیا نہیں دیکھتے کہ اس نے پانچ سو کی ادائیگی کو ون تھہرایا ہے۔ کلمہ علی کے ساتھ ذکر کر کے جو معاوضہ کے لئے ہے۔ حالانکہ ادائیگی عوض ہونے کے لائق نہیں مقروض پر اس کے واجب ہونے کی وجہ سے پس اس کا و بنود عدم کے درجہ میں ہو گیا اور مطلق ابر اور ہ گیا۔ لہذا ہزار کا قرضہ نہیں کوٹے گا۔ جیسے اگر وہ پہلے ابراء کو ذکر کرے۔ طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ ابراء شرط کے ساتھ مفید ہے تو شرط کے فوت ہونے سے ابراء بھی فوت ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے کل کے دن پانچ سکی ادائیگی کو پہلے ذکر کیا ہے جوغرض صحیح ہو سکتی ہے۔ اس کے افلاس سے ڈرتے ہوئے یا اس سے زیادہ سود مند تجارت کا وسیلہ اپناتے ہوئے اور کلمہ علی اگر چہ معاوضہ کے لئے ہے تاہم شرط کو مقتل ہے مقابلہ کے معنی پائے جانے کی وجہ سے تو شرط ہی پر محمول کیا جائے گا۔ معاوضہ پر حمل کے متعذر ہونے کے وقت اس کے تصرف کو صحیح کرنے کی غرض سے یا اس لئے کہ یہی متعارف ہے۔ اور ابراء ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جو مقید بشرط ہو سکتی ہیں۔ اگر چہ متعلق بشرط نہیں ہوسکتی اور ابراء مقدم کی تخریج ہم انشاء اللہ آگے بتائیں گے۔
تشریح قوله ومن له على اخرا الخ - زید کے عمر و پر ہزار درہم تھے اس نے عمر سے کہا کہ تو مجھے کل پانچ سودیدے اس شرط پر کہ باقی پانچ سو سے تو بری ہو جائے گا۔ عمرو نے ایسا ہی کیا تو وہ پانچ سو سے بری ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر اس نے کل کے دن ادا نہ کئے تو طرفین کے نزدیک بری نہ ہوگا۔ امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اس صورت میں بھی بری ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ابراء مطلق تو وہ ادا کرے یا نہ کرے ہر صورت بری ہو جائے گا۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتے کہ اس نے پانچ سو درہم کی ادائیگی کو ابراء کا عوض ٹھہرایا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ اس عوض پر تو زیادتی سے بری ہے۔ حالانکہ ادا ئیگی عوض ہونے کے لائق نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ ادا کرنا تو خود مقروض پر واجب تھا پس اس کا یہ کہنا اور نہ کہنا برابر ہوا اور مطلق ابراء رہ گیا۔ لہذا ہزار درہم کا قرضہ عود نہیں کرے گا۔ جیسے اگر وہ ابراء کو مقدم کر کے یوں کہے ابرأتك على خمس مائة من الف على ان تؤدى غدًا خمس مائة من الف كياس صورت میں وہ بالا تفاق کل سے بری ہو جاتا ہے ادا کرے یا نہ کرے۔ قوله ذكره بكلمة على الخ - بكلمة علی میں باء برائے مقابلہ ہے جیسے بعت هذا بھذا میں ہے۔ پس عبارت کے معنی یہ ہوۓ۔حیث ذکر اداء الخمس مائة بمقابلة كلمة على التي للمعاوضہ بعض حضرات نے یاء کو برائے الصاق مان کر دخول فی الا د امراء لیا ہے اور عبارت کی توجیہ یوں کی ہے۔ حيث ذره بـكـلـمـة عـلـى اى في المعنى ولا ففي اللفظ دخل كلمة علي في الابراء دون الاداء اہ لیکن پہلی توجیہہ کی صورت میں اس محل کی کوئی ضرورت نہیں رہتی۔ کی ۔ ( نتائج)
قوله ولهما ان هذا ... الخ - طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہاں ابراء مطلق نہیں بلکہ شرط ادا کے ساتھ مقید ہے۔ تو فوات شرط سے ابراء بھی جاتا رہے گا۔ کیونکہ اس نے کل کے روز پانچ سو کی ادائیگی سے ابتداء کی ہے۔ اور اس میں اُس کی غرض صحیح ہوسکتی ہے کہ ممکن ہے۔ مدعی علیہ کے افلاس کا اندیشہ ہو یا اس نقصان سے زیادہ سود مند تجارت کا وسیلہ ڈھونڈھا ہو۔ اور کلمہ علی گو معاوضہ کے لئے ہے لیکن معنی مقابلہ کی وجہ سے شرط کا بھی احتمال ہے بایں معنی کہ شرط و جزا میں بھی مقابلہ کے معنی پائے جاتے ہیں تو شرط ہی پر محمول کیا جائے گا جب کہ معاوضہ کے معنی میں لینا ہا ہیں وجہ ناممکن ہے کہ عاقل بالغ کا فعل صیح نہیں رہتا۔ یا اس لئے کہ ایسے موقع میں عر فا شرط ہی کے معنی لئے جاتے ہیں۔ قوله والا براء مما يتقيد.... الخ - سوال کا جواب ہے۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ براء کو شرط پر معلق کرنا بالاتفاق باطل ہے جیسے غریم یا کفیل سے یہ کہے۔ اذا اديت الى خمس مائة فانت برئ من الباقی اور تنقید بالشرط تعلیق ہی ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ابراء مقید بشرط ہو سکتی ہے۔ اگر چہ معلق بشرط نہیں ہوتی جیسے حوالہ کا حال ہے کہ محیل کی برکت سلامتی کی شرط کے ساتھ
One person owes another one thousand dirhams. He said: 'Give me five hundred of them tomorrow, on the condition that you are free from the remainder.' He did so, so he becomes free. And if he does not pay five hundred tomorrow, the one thousand returns upon him. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: 'They do not return upon him because it is an absolute discharge (ibra'). Do you not see that he made the payment of five hundred a compensation (iwad) since he mentioned it with the word 'ala', which is for exchange? Payment is not suitable as compensation because it is already obligatory upon him. Thus, its existence is like non-existence, and the discharge remains absolute, so it does not return, just as if he began with the discharge.' The argument of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that this is a discharge restricted by a condition, so it is lost with the loss of the condition. Because he began with the payment of five hundred on the morrow, and that can be a valid purpose—fearing his insolvency or seeking a more profitable trade. The word 'ala', even if it is for exchange, can also bear the meaning of condition due to the presence of the idea of correspondence in it. So it is interpreted as condition when interpretation as exchange is impossible, to validate his action. Or because this is the customary usage. And discharge is among those things that can be restricted by condition, even though it cannot be suspended by condition, like hawala (transfer). The issue of beginning with discharge will come later, God willing. Translation: Someone owes another one thousand dirhams. He said: 'Give me five hundred of them tomorrow on the condition that you are free from the remainder.' He did so, so he becomes free. If he does not give five hundred dirhams tomorrow, then the one thousand dirhams return upon him. This is the statement of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Imam Abu Yusuf says that they do not return because this is an absolute discharge. Do you not see that he made the payment of five hundred a compensation, mentioning it with the word 'ala' which is for exchange, while payment is not suitable as compensation because it is already obligatory upon the debtor. Thus, its existence becomes like non-existence, and the discharge remains absolute. Therefore, the one thousand debt does not return, just as if he mentioned the discharge first. The proof of the two is that this is a discharge restricted by a condition, so with the loss of the condition the discharge is also lost. Because he began with the payment of five hundred on the morrow, which can be a valid purpose—fearing his insolvency or seeking a more profitable trade. The word 'ala', even if for exchange, can also bear the meaning of condition due to the presence of the idea of correspondence, so it is interpreted as condition when interpretation as exchange is impossible, to validate his action. Or because this is the customary usage. And discharge is among things that can be restricted by condition, even though it cannot be suspended by condition, like the case of hawala. The derivation of prior discharge we will mention later if God wills. Commentary on his statement 'And whoever owes another...' - Zayd owed Amr one thousand dirhams. He said to Amr: 'Give me five hundred tomorrow on condition that you are free from the remaining five hundred.' Amr did so, so he is free from five hundred. If he does not pay on the morrow, according to the two he is not free. According to Abu Yusuf, even in this case he is free because the discharge is absolute; whether he pays or not, he is free in all cases. Do you not see that he made the payment of five hundred dirhams a compensation for the discharge, saying: 'on condition that you are free from the excess'? While payment is not suitable as compensation because payment was already obligatory upon him. So his saying and not saying are equal, and the absolute discharge remains. Therefore, the one thousand dirham debt does not return, just as if he preceded the discharge saying: 'I discharge you from one thousand on condition that you pay five hundred tomorrow'—in that case he is free by consensus whether he pays or not. His statement 'he mentioned it with the word 'ala'...' - In the word 'ala', the ba is for correspondence, as in 'I sold this for this.' So the meaning of the phrase is: when he mentioned the payment of five hundred in exchange with the word 'ala' which is for exchange. Some scholars consider the ya as for conjunction and take 'with the word 'ala'' as entering into the discharge rather than the payment. But with the first interpretation there is no need for this. His statement 'and the two argue that this...' - The argument of the two is that here the discharge is not absolute but restricted by the condition of payment. So with the loss of the condition, the discharge also lapses. Because he began with the payment of five hundred on the morrow, and there can be a valid purpose in that, such as fear of the debtor's insolvency or seeking a more profitable trade. The word 'ala', even if for exchange, can also bear the meaning of condition because the idea of correspondence is present. So it is interpreted as condition when interpretation as exchange is impossible, to validate his action. Or because this is the customary usage. His statement 'and discharge is among things that can be restricted by condition...' - This is an answer to a question. The question is that suspending discharge on a condition is invalid by consensus, e.g., saying to the debtor or guarantor: 'If you pay me five hundred, you are free from the remainder,' and restricting by condition is suspension. The essence of the answer is that discharge can be restricted by condition even though it cannot be suspended by condition, like the case of hawala where the transferor's acquittal is conditioned on the soundness (of the transfer).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a conditional discharge of debt. The scenario: debtor owes 1000 dirhams, creditor says 'Pay me 500 tomorrow and you are free from the remainder.' If paid, all agree he is free. If not paid, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two') hold that the original debt of 1000 dirhams returns, because the discharge was conditional on payment. Abu Yusuf holds that the discharge is absolute; the payment was mentioned as compensation (iwad) but since payment is already obligatory, it is not valid compensation, so the discharge stands unconditionally, and the debt does not return. The text discusses the linguistic meaning of the word 'ala' (upon/on condition) and whether it indicates exchange or condition. The commentator supports the view of the two, explaining that such discharge can be restricted by condition, analogous to hawala (transfer of debt). The dispute highlights a key Hanafi principle: ibra' (discharge) is generally unconditional, but when clearly conditioned, it may be treated as conditional to validate the transaction.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): A legal discharge or release from a debt or obligation, where the creditor relinquishes the right to claim.
على (ala): A preposition meaning 'on' or 'upon', used in contracts to indicate condition or exchange. In the disputed phrase, it is ambiguous between indicating compensation (exchange) and condition.
شرط (shart): A condition attached to a contract or legal act, whose fulfillment affects the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a creditor says 'Pay me 500 dirhams tomorrow and you are free from the 1000 dirham debt' and the debtor fails to pay, what is the effect?
A) The debtor is still free from the entire debt.
B) The original debt of 1000 dirhams returns.
C) The debtor is free from 500 dirhams only.
D) The creditor can claim 500 dirhams only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ibra' can be conditional, and if the condition fails, the discharge is void and the original debt revives, according to the relied-upon position.

Ibra' with Explicit Condition: The Hanafi Ruling
Explains ruling on ibra' when condition is explicitly stated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ibra' with an explicit condition and explain why it is invalid based on the dual nature of ibra'.

Core Idea
Ibra' with an explicit condition is invalid because it combines isqat and tamlik, and tamlik cannot be conditional.

This lesson examines the fifth case of ibra' (remission of debt) where the creditor explicitly links the remission to a future event. The Hanafi school rules that such an explicit condition invalidates the ibra', and the reason lies in the unique dual nature of ibra' as both isqat and tamlik. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying ibra' in financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ibra' (remission of debt) with an explicit condition (e.g., 'if you repay half' or 'when you give birth'). The Hanafi ruling is that this ibra' is batil (invalid).
Contrast with ibra' without explicit condition, which is sahih (valid).
2
Understand the dual nature of ibra'
Ibra' has two aspects: isqat (dropping of a right) and tamlik (transfer of ownership). Isqat does not require acceptance and can be conditional (like divorce). Tamlik requires acceptance and cannot be conditional (like sale).
This dual nature is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Compare isqat and tamlik regarding conditions
Isqat can be suspended on conditions (e.g., 'when you enter the house, you are divorced'). Tamlik cannot be conditional (e.g., 'I sell you this if it rains' is invalid). Since ibra' includes tamlik, the explicit condition invalidates the tamlik aspect.
If the condition is implicit or not explicit, the ibra' is valid because the tamlik aspect is not violated.
4
Apply the rule to explicit and non-explicit conditions
If the creditor says 'I forgive your debt' (no condition), it is valid. If he says 'I forgive your debt if you pay me tomorrow,' it is invalid because of the explicit condition. The presence of words like 'if' or 'when' triggers the invalidity.
This is the practical difference: explicit condition invalidates, implicit condition does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والخامس اذا قال ... الخ - پانچویں صورت یہ ہے کہ اس نے صریح شرط بیان کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ اگر یا جب یا مہر گاہ تو مجھے پانچ ہے اور وجہ یہ ہے کہ اسقاط ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ قبول کرنے پر موقوف نہیں ہوتا اور اس میں تملیک کے معنی بھی ہیں۔ یہاں تک کہ رد کرنے سے رد ہو جاتا ہے اور تملیک بیع وغیرہ شرط کے ساتھ جائز نہیں اور اسقاط کی تعلیق عتاق و طلاق وغیرہ شرط کے ساتھ جائز ہے۔ پس ابراء جو دونوں معنوں پر مشتمل ہے اس کی بابت ہم نے کہا کہ جب شرط کی تصریح نہ ہو تو صحیح ہے اور جب شرط کی تصریح ہو تو صحیح نہیں۔ عملا بالشبهين۔
And the fifth: when he said... etc. - The fifth case is that he stated an explicit condition saying: if or when or if she gives birth, then I have five [i.e., remission of five]. And the reason is that [ibra'] is isqat (remission) to the extent that it is not dependent on acceptance, and it also has the meaning of tamlik (ownership transfer) to the extent that it is voidable by rejection. Tamlik in sale etc. is not valid with condition, but ta'liq (suspension) of isqat in emancipation, divorce, etc. is valid with condition. Thus ibra', which combines both meanings, we said: when the condition is not explicit it is valid, and when the condition is explicit it is not valid, acting on both aspects.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth case of ibra' (remission of debt) where the creditor explicitly conditions the remission on a future event. The commentator explains that ibra' has a dual nature: it is isqat (dropping of a right) and tamlik (transfer of ownership). Isqat does not require acceptance and can be suspended on conditions like divorce and emancipation. Tamlik (like sale) cannot be conditional. Since ibra' combines both, when the condition is explicit (using words like 'if' or 'when'), the tamlik aspect is invalid, so the entire ibra' is invalid. If the condition is implicit or not explicit, ibra' is valid. This is a Hanafi ruling based on the dual nature of ibra'.

Key Terms
ابراء (ibra'): Remission of debt; a legal act that drops a claim from the debtor's liability.
اسقاط (isqat): Dropping or removing a right; does not require acceptance and can be conditional.
تملیک (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; requires acceptance and cannot be conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is ibra' (remission of debt) invalid when an explicit condition is stated?
A) Because ibra' is pure isqat and isqat cannot be conditional.
B) Because ibra' combines isqat and tamlik, and tamlik cannot be conditional.
C) Because ibra' is a contract and all contracts are invalid with conditions.
D) Because the condition renders the ibra' ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The dual nature of ibra' (isqat and tamlik) determines its validity when conditioned explicitly; explicit condition invalidates due to tamlik.

Extreme Necessity Does Not Invalidate Transactions
Talil: why idtirar is not ikrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between legal coercion (ikrah) and compulsion (idtirar) and apply the Hanafi ruling that transactions under idtirar are valid.

Core Idea
Extreme hunger is not coercion; the ability to seek legal remedy preserves validity.

When does a pressured transaction become invalid? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between coercion (ikrah) and mere compulsion (idtirar). This passage explains that even someone starving who buys food at an unfair price still has a valid contract — because they could theoretically seek legal recourse. Let's unpack this key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What transaction is being discussed? A person buys food at an exorbitant price or sells an item for food due to extreme hunger. The ruling: the transaction is valid (nafiz).
The ruling is 'sahih' — valid and enforceable.
2
Locate the legal proof (dalil)
The proof is not a quoted text but a logical reasoning (talil): the person is not coerced because they can establish proof (iqamat bayyinah) to seek legal remedy. True coercion requires inability to resist; here, the person retains that ability.
This reasoning is based on the Hanafi definition of ikrah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link between the reason and the ruling: if the person could resist by legal means, then the external pressure does not remove legal capacity. Hence, the contract stands.
The 'illa' (effective cause) of invalidity in coercion is loss of free choice (ikhtiyar); idtirar does not remove free choice entirely.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may consider extreme necessity as duress that invalidates contracts. The Hanafi position strictly limits invalidity to genuine coercion.
This is a legitimate scholarly difference.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi (operative) position is the stated one: idtirar does not invalidate. This is from Zahir al-Riwayah and relied upon by later Hanafi jurists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قـولـه لأنه ليس بمكره ... الخ -مکرہ نہ ہونا اس لئے ہے کہ وہ اقامت بینہ کے ذریعہ سے اس کو دفع کر سکتا ہے۔ صرف اتنی بات ہے کہ یہاں ایک طرح کا اضطرار نفاذ تصرف سے مانع نہیں ہوتا۔ چنانچہ اگر کوئی شخص انتہائی بھوک سے معطر ہو کر کھانے کی چیز گراں قیمت پر خرید لے یا اپنی کوئی چیز طعام کے عوض میں فروخت کر دے تو اس کا تصرف نافذ ہوتا ہے۔ اگر چہ وہ اس کی طرف مضطر ہے۔
His statement 'because he is not coerced' etc. - Not being coerced is because he can repel it by establishing proof. The only matter is that a kind of compulsion (idtirar) here does not prevent the validity of the transaction. For example, if a person, extremely hungry and compelled, buys food at an exorbitant price or sells his own item in exchange for food, his transaction is valid even though he is compelled.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage clarifies that coercion (ikrah) that invalidates transactions is only when one is forced under threat of harm with no legal recourse. Here, extreme hunger (idtirar) does not constitute such coercion because the person can theoretically establish a case or seek legal remedy. The commentator gives an example: even if someone is starving and buys food at an inflated price or sells something for food, the transaction is valid (nafiz). This is a Hanafi ruling: necessity does not invalidate contracts unless there is actual coercion with inability to resist. The phrase 'معطر' in the OCR may be a misprint for 'مضطر' (compelled by necessity).

Key Terms
مکرہ (mukrah): A person coerced under threat of harm with no legal means of escape; such a person's transactions may be invalid.
اضطرار (idtirar): Compulsion or extreme necessity (like hunger) that drives a person to act but does not remove legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a transaction made under extreme hunger valid?
A) Because hunger is a form of coercion that the law recognizes.
B) Because the person can still establish proof and seek legal remedy, so it is not true coercion.
C) Because the sale of food is always valid regardless of circumstances.
D) Because the buyer has no choice but to buy.

Key Takeaway
Extreme necessity (idtirar) does not invalidate transactions in Hanafi law; the ability to seek legal recourse preserves validity.

Joint Debt: Options After a Partial Settlement
Ruling on joint debt when one partner settles with a cloth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to a co-creditor when one partner settles a joint debt with a specific item, and apply the condition of guarantee that removes those options.

Core Idea
A co-creditor may share in what the other receives from the joint debtor unless the recipient guarantees a quarter of the debt.

This passage from Al-Hidayah explains the rights of joint creditors. When one creditor accepts a payment in kind (e.g., a cloth) to settle his share, the other creditor is not left out. He retains options to either take a share of the item or pursue the original debtor. The underlying principle is that the receipt of payment is considered an increase (ziyadah) attached to the original right, much like the offspring of a jointly owned animal. An exception occurs when the settling partner guarantees a portion of the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its elements
The case: two co-creditors, one settles his half of the debt with a cloth. The ruling: the other co-creditor has two options – take half the cloth or pursue the debtor for his half. There is an exception: if the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt, the options are restricted.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is analogical reasoning (qiyas): the increase from receipt is compared to offspring or fruit of joint property, which are shared. The text states 'فَيَصِيرُ كَزِيَادَةِ الْوَلَدِ وَالثَّمَرَةِ' (it becomes like the increase of a child or fruit).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof works)
The receipt of cash (or cloth) adds value compared to the debt outstanding. This added value (ziyadah) reverts to the original right, so the co-creditor can share. The analogy to child/fruit shows that any increase to joint property is shared.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Later commentators (Sahib Nihaya, Sahib Kifaya) disagree on whether the exception ('إِلَّا أَنْ يَضْمَنَ') negates the option to pursue the debtor or the option to take half the cloth.
The mufta bihi is that it negates the option to take half the cloth.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a joint debt, if one partner settles his share by accepting a car, the other may either take half the car or pursue the debtor for his half of the money, unless the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشترک دین کا بیان دین دو آدمیوں میں مشترک ہو ایک نے اپنے حصہ دین کے بدلے کپڑے پر صلح کر لی تو دوسرے شریک کو اختیار ہے کہ نصف دین لے یا نصف کپڑا لے ۔ مگر جب شریک ربع دین کا ضامن بن جائے (فصل فِي الدَيْنِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ) وَإِذَا كَانَ الدَيْنُ بَيْنَ شَرِيكَيْنِ فَصَالَحَ اَحَدُهُمَا مِنْ نَصِيبِهِ عَلَى ثَوْبِ فَشَرِيْكُهُ بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَتْبَعَ الَّذِى عَلَيْهِ الدَّيْنُ بِنِصْفِهِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَ نِصْفَ التَّوْبِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَضْمَنَ لَهُ شَرِيكَهُ رَبِّعُ الدَّيْنِ وَأَصْلُ هَذَا أَنْ الدَيْـنَ الْمُشْتَرَكَ بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ إِذَا قَبَضَ أَحَدُهُمَا شَيْئًا مِنْهُ فَلِصَاحِبِهِ أَنْ يُشَارِكَهُ فِي الْمَقْبُوضِ لأَنَّهُ ازْدَادَ بِالْقَبْضِ إِذْ مَالِيَةُ الدَيْنِ بِاعْتِبَارِ عَاقِبَةِ الْقَبْضِ وَهَذِهِ الزِيَادَةُ رَاجِعَةٌ إِلَى أَصْلِ الْحَقِّ فَيَصِيرُ كَزِيَادَةِ الْوَلَدِ وَالثَّمَرَةِ فَلَه حَقُ الْمُشَارَكَةِ وَلَكِنَّه قَبْلَ الْمُشَارَكَةِ بَاقٍ عَلَى مِلْكِ الْقَابِضِ لِاَنَّ الْعَيْنَ غَيْرُ الدَّيْنِ حَقِيقَةً وَقَدَ قَبَضَهَ بَدَلًا عَنْ حَقِهِ فَيَمْلِكُهُ حَتَّى يَنْفُذَ تَصَرُّفَهُ فِيهِ وَيَضْمَنُ لِشَرِيكِهِ حِصَتْه وَالدَّيْنُ الْمُشْتَرَكُ أَنْ يَكُونَ وَاجِبًا بِسَبَبِ مُتَّحِدٍ كَتَمَنِ الْمَبِيعِ إِذَا كَانَ صَفَقَةً وَاحِدَةً وَثَمَنِ الْمَالِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ وَالْمَوْرُوثِ بَيْنَهُمَا وَقِيمَةُ الْمُسْتَهَلَكِ الْمُشْتَرَكِ فَإِذَا عَرَفْتَ هَذَا نَقُولُ فِي مَسْأَلَةِ الْكِتَابِ لَهُ أَنْ يَتَّبِعَ الَّذِي اپنے حصہ کی طرف سے کپڑے پر صلح کرلے تو اس کے شریک کو اختیار ہے۔ چاہے اس کا دامن گیر ہو جس پر قرضہ ہے۔ اپنے نصف حصہ کے لئے اور چاہے آدھا کپڑا لے لے ۔ لا یہ کہ ضامن ہو اس کے لئے اس کا شریک چوتھائی قرض کا اور اصل اس باب میں یہ ہے کہ جو قرضہ دو میں مشترک ہو اور ان میں سے کوئی ایک کچھ وصول کر لے تو دوسرے کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ مقبوض میں اس کا شریک ہو جائے۔ کیونکہ اس نے وصول کرنے میں زیادتی لے لی اس لئے کہ قرضہ کی مالیت انجام کار وصول ہو جانے کے اعتبار سے ہے۔ اور یہ زیادتی اصل حق کی جانب راجع ہے تو بچہ اور پھل کی زیادتی کے مثل ہو گئی۔ پس ہر شریک کو حق مشارکت - مشارکت سے پہلے وہ ملک قابض پر باقی ہے کیونکہ عین حقیقۂ غیر دین ہے اور اس نے اپنے حق کے عوض میں لیا ہے تو وہ اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ یہاں تک کہ اس میں اس کا تصرف نافذ ہوگا۔ اور اپنے شریک کے لئے اس کے حصہ کا ضامن ہوگا۔ اور مشترک قرضہ وہ ہے جو سبب متحد سے واجب ہو جیسے مبیع کا شمن جب کہ بصفقہ واحدہ ہو۔ اور جیسے مشترک یا مورث مال کا شمن اور جیسے تلف شدہ مشترک مال کی قیمت۔ جب یہ معلوم ہو گیا تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ مسئلہ کتاب میں شریک کو اختیار ہے کہ اس کا دامن گیر ہو جس پر اصل ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کا حصہ بذمہ مدیون باقی ہے۔ کیونکہ قابض نے صرف اپنا حصہ وصول کر لیا ہے لیکن اس کو مشارکت کا حق ہے۔ اور چاہے آدھا کپڑا لے لے۔ کیونکہ اس کو مشارکت کا حق ہے مگر یہ کہ اس کے لئے اس کا شریک چوتھائی قرض کا ضامن ہو جائے۔ کیونکہ اس کا حق اسی میں ہے۔ اگر ان میں کوئی اپنا نصف قرضہ وصول کر چکا تو اس کا شریک مقبوض میں شریک ہوسکتا ہے۔ پھر دونوں شریک باقی مقروض سے وصول کریں اس لئے کہ جب مقبوض میں دونوں شریک ہوئے تو باقی ضرور شرکت پر ہو گا۔ تشریح قوله وَإذَا كان الدين .... الخ قرضہ دو شریکوں کے درمیان مشترک ہے ان میں سے ایک شریک نے اپنے حصہ کی طرف سے کسی کپڑے پر گا۔ قوله الا ان يضمن له .... الخ - صاحب نہایہ اور اورشیخ اتقانی نے کہا ہے کہ یہ فشریکہ بالخياراہ سے استثناء ہے۔ صاحب نہایہ نے بھی اپنے قول فانه لاخيار لشريكه في ابتاع الغريم سے اسی کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے۔ اور اس کو صاحب کفایہ نے احسن کہا ہے۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ غیر مصالح شریک کو مذکور اختیار ہے۔ مگر یہ کہ اس کا شریک ربع دین کا ضامن ہو جائے کہ اس صورت میں اس کی اختیار نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن تقریر کافی کا ظاہر اور صاحب کفایہ کی صراحت یہ ہے کہ یہ ان شاء اخذ نصف الثوبہ سے استثناء ہے۔ اس لئے کہ جب شریک اس کے لئے ربع دین کا ضامن ہو گیا تو اب اس کو نصف ثوب میں شرکت کی ولایت نہیں رہی۔ اور یہ بھی ممکن ہے کہ ان شاء اتبع الذى عَلَيْهِ الدين بنصف سے استثناء ہو۔ اس لئے کہ جب شریک اس کے لئے نصف مقبوض کا ضامن ہو گیا تو اب اس کو نصف دین واپس لینے کی ولایت نہیں رہی۔ بل يرجع بربعہ۔ قوله واصل هذا...... الخ - صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس باب میں قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جو قرضہ دو شریکوں میں مشترک ہو اور ان میں سے ایک شریک کچھ حصہ وصول کرے تو دوسرے شریک کو اختیار ہوتا ہے چاہے وہ اس وصول کردہ حصہ میں شریک ہو جائے اور چاہے اصل مدیون سے اپنے حصہ کا مطالبہ کرے۔ وصول کردہ حصہ میں شریک ہو جانے کا اختیار اس لئے ہے کہ اس نے وصولیابی میں زیادتی لے لی۔ بایں معنی کہ نقد کو ادھار پر ایک قسم کی مزیت ہے اس لئے کہ قرض کی مالیت انجام کار وصول ہو جانے کے لحاظ سے ہے تو جس نے ابھی وصول نہیں کیا۔ اس کے حصہ میں گو یا مالیت نہیں ہے۔ اور وصول کنندہ نے بالفعل اس کی مالیت پالی۔ پس شرکت کے باوجود اس نے زیادہ پالیا اور یہ زیادتی چونکہ اصل حق کی جانب راجع ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا۔ جیسے مشتر کہ باندی سے بچہ یا مشترک درخت سے پھل پیدا ہو کہ اس میں ہر شریک کو مشارکت کا حق ہوتا ہے۔ قوله ولكنه قبل المشاركة... الخ - یعنی ایک شریک نے جو کچھ وصول کیا ہے وہ دوسرے شریک اختیار شرکت سے پہلے وصول کنندہ ہی کی ملک پر باقی ہے۔ کیونکہ اس نے جو کچھ وصول کیا وہ مین ہو گیا۔ اور عین و دین میں حقیقت مغایرت ہے مگر اس نے چونکہ اس مین کو اپنے حق کے عوض میں لیا ہے اس لئے وہ اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر وصول کردہ میں اس نے ہبہ وغیرہ کا کوئی تصرف کیا تو نافذ ہوگا اور اپنے شریک کے لئے اس کے حصہ کے بقدر ضامن ہو گا۔ اسی لئے مسئلہ میں کہا ہے۔ الا ان يضمن له شريكه ربع الدين قوله والدين المشترك... الخ - دین مشترک اس کو کہتے ہیں جو سبب متحد کے ذریعہ واجب ہو ۔ جیسے اس مبیع کا شمن جس کی بیع بصفقہ واحدہ ہوئی ہو۔ مثلاً دونوں نے اپنا اپنا غلام ملا کر ایک ہی بولی میں دو ہزار کے عوض فروخت کیا تو یہ ثمن مشترک ہو جائے گا۔ جو مشتری پر قرضہ ہے اور
Statement of joint debt: When a debt is shared between two partners, and one of them settles from his share with a cloth, then his partner has the option: if he wishes, he may pursue the debtor for his half, and if he wishes, he may take half of the cloth, unless his partner guarantees for him a quarter of the debt. The principle is that when the joint debt is between two, and one of them receives something from it, his partner may share in what was received because he gained by the receipt, since the value of the debt is based on the consequence of receipt. This increase reverts to the origin of the right, so it becomes like the increase of a child or fruit. Thus he has the right of participation, but before participation it remains in the ownership of the recipient because the tangible item is different from the debt in reality, and he received it as a substitute for his right, so he owns it such that his disposition in it is valid, and he guarantees to his partner his share. Joint debt is that which is obligatory by a single cause, like the price of the sold item when it is a single transaction, the price of jointly owned or inherited property, and the value of jointly consumed property. When you understand this, we say in the case of the book: he may pursue the original debtor because his share remains in the debtor's liability, since the recipient received only his share, but he has the right of participation. And if he wishes, he may take half of the cloth because he has the right of participation, unless his partner guarantees for him a quarter of the debt, because his right is in that. He said: If one of them recovers half of his share of the debt, his partner may share in what he received for what we said. Then both will return to the debtor for the remainder because when they shared in the receipt, the remainder must be subject to partnership. (Translation of subsequent Urdu follows similar pattern: detailed explanation of the Arabic text.)
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Joint Debt (al-Dayn al-Mushtarak) presents the ruling when one of two joint creditors settles his share of the debt with a specific item (a cloth). The other creditor then has two options: either pursue the debtor for his half of the original debt, or take half of the cloth received. However, if the settling creditor guarantees a quarter of the debt (or half, as some interpretations) to the other, then the other loses the option. The underlying principle is that when one partner receives something for the joint debt, the other has the right to participate in that receipt because the receipt adds value (since cash in hand is better than a debt owed). This increase is considered like an increase to the original right, analogous to offspring of a jointly owned slave or fruit of a tree. Before the other partner exercises this right to participate, the received item is owned solely by the recipient, so his transactions with it are valid, but he is then liable to his partner for the latter's share. The passage also defines joint debt as arising from a single cause (e.g., a single sale price, compensation for jointly owned property). The commentary includes discussions from other scholars (Sahib Nihaya, Sahib Kifaya) on the precise point of exception.

Key Terms
دين مشترك (dayn mushtarak): Joint debt; a debt owed by a single debtor to multiple creditors from a single cause.
مشاركة (musharaka): Right to share in what the co-creditor has received from the debtor.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; the settling partner guarantees a portion of the debt to the other, extinguishing the participation right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In joint debt, when one partner settles his share with a cloth, the other partner may either take half the cloth or pursue the debtor for his half. What condition removes these options?
A) If the debtor pays the remaining half directly.
B) If the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt to the other.
C) If the other partner explicitly waives his right.
D) If the cloth is destroyed before delivery.

Key Takeaway
A co-creditor may participate in what his partner receives from a joint debtor, unless the partner guarantees a quarter of the debt, which removes the option to share the received item.

Linguistic and Technical Definition of Wadi'ah
Definition, linguistic root, and legal nature of wadi'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah linguistically and technically, identify its conditions for validity, and distinguish it from the broader concept of amanah.

Core Idea
Wadi'ah is a trust by contract; the trustee is not liable without negligence.

This lesson opens the Book of Wadi'ah (Deposits) in Al-Hidayah. The author first explains why the chapter follows Mudarabah: both involve trust (amanah). Then he defines wadi'ah, tracing its linguistic root from 'wada'a' (to leave or preserve) and distinguishing it from other contracts like 'ariyah, hibah, and ijarah. The lesson clarifies the technical definition, the conditions for a valid deposit, and the critical difference between wadi'ah and general amanah. Understanding this foundation is essential before studying the detailed rulings of deposits.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic root of wadi'ah
Identify the Arabic root wada'a and its meanings: to leave, to preserve. Note the Quranic verse 'ma wadda'aka rabbuka' and the hadith proving usage of the verb.
The root is shared with ideas of leaving and preserving.
2
Understand the technical definition
Wadi'ah is a contract where one person entrusts property to another for safekeeping, making the trustee an ameen (trustee) without liability for loss unless negligent.
The depositor is muudi', the trustee is muuda' or ameen.
3
Identify conditions for validity
The deposited property must be capable of possession. Depositing a runaway slave or an item fallen into a river is invalid because possession cannot be established.
This condition is mentioned in the text.
4
Distinguish wadi'ah from amanah
Amanah is broader: it can arise without a contract (e.g., a windblown cloth enters a house). Wadi'ah always requires a contract of safekeeping and does not create liability upon return.
The passage notes a dispute with the author of Nata'ij on this point.

فائدہ ودیعت اور امانت میں فرق یہ ہے کہ امانت عام ہے اور ودیعت خاص ہے۔ کیونکہ ودیعت میں غیر سے حفاظت کرانا قصد ا مطلوب ہوتا ہے اور امانت کبھی بلا قصد بھی ہوتی ہے۔ مثلاً ہوانے کسی کا کپڑا اڑا کر دوسرے کے گھر میں ڈال دیا تو وہ کپڑا اس کے پاس امانت ہے۔ نیز ودیعت بذریعہ عقد ہوتی ہے۔ امانت میں عقد ضروری نہیں۔ پھر ودیعت میں اعادہ الی الوفاق سے ضمان لازم نہیں ہوتا۔ بخلاف امانت کے کہ اس میں ضمان واجب ہوتا ہے۔ لیکن صاحب نتائج نے اس طویل بحث کی ہے۔ جس کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ ان دونوں میں مبانیت ہے۔
The Book of Wadi'ah. His statement: Book ... Wadi'ah and mudarabah share in being trusts, so after mudarabah he brings wadi'ah. Then wadi'ah is a trust without ownership, and 'ariyah is a transfer of benefit without compensation as a trust, and hibah is pure transfer of ownership without compensation, and ijarah involves transfer of benefit with compensation. So the author brings after wadi'ah the 'ariyah, hibah, and ijarah in this order, which is an ascent from the lower to the higher. Benefit: Safeguarding a trust leads to happiness in both worlds, and betrayal leads to misery. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Trustworthiness brings wealth and betrayal brings poverty.' And in the proverb: 'Trustworthiness made kings remain as kings, and betrayal made kings become subjects.' His statement: al-wadi'ah ... Wadi'ah is linguistically a fa'ilah form in the sense of maf'ulah, derived from wada'a meaning absolute leaving. Allah said: 'Your Lord has not forsaken you nor does He hate you.' In the hadith of Ibn Abbas: The Prophet said: 'People will desist from abandoning the congregations or Allah will seal their hearts, then they will be written among the heedless.' From this it is known that what Shammar said, that the grammarians claimed the Arabs have made the masdar of yada'u extinct, is false, because the Messenger of Allah is the most eloquent Arab and his speaking with this word is proven in the hadith. It is called wadi'ah because it is left with the trustee. Also wada'a means to preserve. It is said: wada'a and tawadda'a al-thawb, meaning he preserved the cloth. Al-Ra'i said: 'Praise that brightens noble lineages, by which preserved honor is deposited.' From this is iwda' meaning to deposit something with someone. The Prophet said: 'Faith is a light that Allah deposits in the hearts of believers.' In terminological explanation, iwda' is when one gives another control over safeguarding one's property, whether it is property, provided it is something that can be possessed. So if one deposits a runaway slave or something that fell into the river, this is not valid. Whatever is left in another's custody is called wadi'ah, its plural is wada'i'. Labid ibn Rabi'ah said: 'Wealth and family are only deposits, and inevitably one day the deposits will be returned.' The one who leaves is called muudi' (with kasr), and the one with whom it is left is called muuda' and ameen. Benefit: The difference between wadi'ah and amanah is that amanah is general and wadi'ah is specific, because in wadi'ah the intent is to seek preservation from others, while amanah may occur without intent. For example, if a wind blew someone's cloth into another's house, that cloth is amanah with him. Also wadi'ah is by contract, amanah does not require contract. Then in wadi'ah, returning it to the owner does not necessitate liability, unlike amanah where liability is obligatory. But the author of Nata'ij has discussed this at length, the summary of which is that there is mutual contradiction between them.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Wadi'ah (Deposits). It begins by explaining why the author places this chapter after Mudarabah: both involve trust (amanah). Then it lists the sequence of subsequent chapters (Ariyah, Hibah, Ijarah) in ascending order from lesser to greater transfer of rights. A benefit is mentioned: the Prophetic hadith encouraging trustworthiness and warning against betrayal. The definition of wadi'ah: linguistically from 'wada'a' (to leave) or 'to preserve'. A hadith from Ibn Abbas is cited to prove that the verb 'wada'a' is used in Arabic, refuting a grammarian's claim that its masdar is obsolete. Technically, wadi'ah is when one entrusts his property to another for safekeeping, valid only if the property can be possessed. Examples: depositing a runaway slave or an item dropped in a river is invalid because possession cannot be established. The depositor is called muudi', the trustee is called muuda' or ameen. Finally, a distinction is made between wadi'ah and amanah: amanah is broader and can arise without a contract, while wadi'ah is specific to a contract of safekeeping. Additionally, in wadi'ah, the trustee is not liable if the item is returned to the owner, whereas in general amanah, liability may apply if the item is lost without negligence? The passage notes a dispute with the author of Nata'ij about this difference.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadīʿah): A contract where one deposits property with another for safekeeping, making the depositee a trustee (ameen) without compensation.
مودع (mūdiʿ (depositor) / mustawdaʿ (depositee)): The depositor is muudiʻ (with kasr on the daal), the depositee is muudaʻ (with fatha) or ameen.
الامانة (al-amānah): Trust; anything held in trust, whether by contract or by circumstance, where the holder is not liable for loss without negligence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To leave or preserve

D) To borrow

Key Takeaway
Wadi'ah is a trust-based contract for safekeeping, derived from the root 'wada'a', and the trustee is only liable if negligent.

8 min
Deposit is a Trust: No Liability Without Negligence
States the ruling that wadi'ah is an amanah and proves it with hadith and necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a depositary for a lost deposit and explain the proof from hadith and rational argument.

Core Idea
The depositary is not liable for loss unless he is treacherous or negligent.

We often leave valuables with others for safekeeping. What happens if they are lost? The Hanafi school rules that a deposit is a trust (amanah), not a guaranty. This principle, rooted in a prophetic hadith and the necessity of social cooperation, protects the depositary from liability unless he oversteps his bounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Pinpoint the exact legal ruling: the depositary is not liable for loss of the deposit. Note the conditional 'unless he causes loss (ghall) or transgresses (ta'addi).'
The Arabic uses 'amānah' which is a key legal category.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof the author cites: a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) and a rational argument (istihsan).
Both dalīl naqlī (textual) and dalīl 'aqlī (rational) are used.
3
Analyze the Hadith's Wajh al-Dalālah
Examine how the hadith proves the ruling. The phrase 'ghayr al-mughill' (non‑treacherous) implies that liability is linked to treachery or causing loss. The negation of liability for others affirms the default trust status.
This is a case of manṭūq (explicit meaning) and mafhūm (implied opposite).
4
Understand the Rational Argument
The author adds an istihsān based on necessity: if depositors were liable, people would stop accepting deposits, harming public interest.
This shows Hanafi reliance on maṣlaḥah (public good) as a secondary source.
5
Evaluate the Hadith Criticism and Response
The text raises a potential weakness in the hadith's chain but responds with corroborating narrations and the principle that vague criticism (jarḥ mubham) is not accepted.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of defending ḥadīth authenticity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ الْوَدِيْعَةُ أَمَانَةٌ فِي يَدِ الْمُودَعِ إِذَا هَلَكَتْ لَمْ يَضْمَنْهَا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ: لَيْسَ عَلَى الْمُسْتَعِيرِ غَيْرِ الْمُغِلِّ ضَمَانٌ، وَلَا عَلَى الْمُسْتَوْدَعِ غَيْرِ الْمُغِلِّ ضَمَانٌ. وَلِأَنَّ بِالنَّاسِ حَاجَةً إِلَى الِاسْتِيدَاعِ، فَلَوْ ضَمَّنَّاهُ امْتَنَعَ النَّاسُ عَنْ قَبُولِ الْوَدَائِعِ فَلَتَعَطَّلَتْ مَصَالِحُهُمْ.

ترجمہ: ودیعت امانت ہوتی ہے مودع کے پاس اگر ہلاک ہو جائے تو ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم کا ارشاد ہے کہ غیر خائن مستعیر پر ضمان نہیں اور نہ غیر خائن مستودع پر۔ اور اس لئے کہ لوگوں کو ودیعت رکھنے کی ضرورت ہوتی ہے۔ پس اگر ہم مستودع کو ضامن ٹھہرائیں تو لوگ ودیعتیں قبول کرنے سے باز رہیں گے اور ان کی مصلحتیں معطل ہو جائیں گی۔ 

تشریح: قوله الوديعة امانة - مودع کے پاس مال ودیعت امانت ہوتا ہے۔ اگر اس کی زیادتی کے بغیر تلف ہو جائے تو تاوان نہ ہوگا۔ یہ حضرت ابو بکر، حضرت علی، ابن مسعودؓ سے مروی ہے اور قاضی شریح، ابراہیم نخعی، ابواوزاعی، سفیان ثوری، اوزاعی، امام مالک، امام شافعی وغیرہ۔ اکثر اہل علم اسی کے قائل ہیں۔ کیونکہ دارقطنی اور بیہقی نے عن عمرو بن شعیب عن ابیہ عن جدہ روایت کی ہے: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: ليس على المستعير غير المغِل ضمان.

سوال: دارقطنی نے کہا ہے کہ یہ حدیث ضعیف ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کے راوی عمرو بن عبد الجبار اور عبیدہ بن حسان ضعیف ہیں۔ ابن حبان نے کتاب الضعفاء میں کہا ہے کہ عبیدہ تو ثقات سے بھی موضوعات روایت کرتا ہے۔

جواب: دار قطنی کا قول مذکور کے عمرو و عبیدہ دونوں ضعیف ہیں جرح مبہم ہے جو غیر مقبول ہے۔ پھر عمرو بن عبد الجبار عبیدہ کا بھتیجا ہے جس کے بارے میں ہمارے علم کے مطابق کسی نے کوئی تعاقب نہیں کیا۔ بجز آنکہ بعض نے صرف یہ کہا ہے: لہ مناکیر۔ رہا عبیدہ بن حسان، امام بخاری نے اپنی تاریخ میں اس کو ذکر کیا ہے اور کوئی جرح نہیں کی۔ علاوہ ازیں روایت مذکورہ کے شواہد موجود ہیں۔ چنانچہ ابن ماجہ کی مرفوع روایت ہے: لا ضمان على وديعة. دارقطنی کی مرفوع روایت ہے: لا ضمان على مؤتمن. جامع سفیان ثوری سے حضرت علی و ابن مسعود سے موقوف روایت ہے: قالا ليس على المؤتمن ضمان. سنن سعید بن منصور میں حضرت جابر بن عبداللہ سے مروی ہے أن أبا بكر قضى في وديعة كانت في جراب فسقت أن لا ضمان فيها. مصنف عبد الرزاق میں حضرت عمر بن الخطاب سے مروی ہے: قال: العارية بمنزلة الوديعة لا ضمان فيها إلا أن يتعدى. حضرت علی سے مروی ہے: ليس على صاحب العارية ضمان و إسناده حسن.
He said: The deposit is a trust in the hand of the depositary. If it is destroyed, he is not liable for it, because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is no liability on the borrower who does not cause loss, and no liability on the depositary who does not cause loss.' And because people have need to deposit, so if we hold him liable, people would refrain from accepting deposits and their interests would be disrupted. Translation: The deposit is a trust with the depositary; if it is destroyed without his excess, he will not compensate. Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no liability on the non-treacherous borrower and no liability on the non-treacherous depositary.' And because people need to deposit; if we hold the depositary liable, people will refrain from accepting deposits and their interests will be disrupted. Commentary: 'He said: The deposit is a trust' – the property deposited is a trust with the depositary. If it is lost without his excess, there is no compensation. This is narrated from Abu Bakr, Ali, Ibn Mas'ud, and it is held by Qadi Shurayh, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, Abu Awzanah, Sufyan al-Thawri, al-Awza'i, Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and others – the majority of scholars. Because al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi narrated from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet said: 'There is no liability on the non-treacherous borrower.' Question: Al-Daraqutni said this hadith is weak because its narrators Amr ibn Abd al-Jabbar and Ubaydah ibn Hassan are weak. Ibn Hibban said in Kitab al-Du'afa that Ubaydah even narrates fabricated reports from trustworthy narrators. Answer: Al-Daraqutni's claim that both are weak is an ambiguous criticism, which is unacceptable. Moreover, Amr ibn Abd al-Jabbar is the nephew of Ubaydah; as far as we know, no one has criticized him except that some said 'he has some odd narrations.' As for Ubaydah ibn Hassan, Imam al-Bukhari mentioned him in his Tarikh and did not criticize him. Besides, there are corroborating narrations for this hadith: Ibn Majah narrated marfu'an: 'There is no liability for a deposit.' Al-Daraqutni narrated marfu'an: 'There is no liability on a trustee.' In the Jami' of Sufyan al-Thawri, from Ali and Ibn Mas'ud, a mawquf narration: 'There is no liability on the trustee.' In Sunan Sa'id ibn Mansur from Jabir ibn Abdillah that Abu Bakr judged concerning a deposit that was in a bag and was lost that there is no liability for it. In Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq from Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'The borrowed item is like the deposit; there is no liability for it unless there is transgression.' From Ali: 'There is no liability on the owner of the borrowed item,' and its chain is good.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof (dalil) from Ashraf al-Hidayah. The matn states clearly that a deposit (wadi'ah) is a trust (amanah) in the hands of the depositary (muda'). If it perishes without the depositary's fault, he is not liable. The proof is the prophetic hadith: 'There is no liability on the borrower (musta'ir) who does not cause loss, nor on the depositary (mustawda') who does not cause loss.' The word 'ghayr al-mughill' means one who does not commit treachery or cause loss. The commentator also gives a rational argument (istihsan): if depositors were held liable, people would stop accepting deposits, causing hardship. The text then addresses an objection about the hadith's chain, defending it by citing multiple corroborating narrations and refuting the criticism as vague. This establishes the ruling within the Hanafi school as mu'tamad.

Key Terms
الْمُسْتَعِيرُ (al-musta'ir): The borrower (of an object for use).
الْمُغِلُّ (al-mughill): One who causes loss or acts treacherously with the property.
ضَمَانٌ (damān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a depositary (muda') liable for a lost deposit?
A) Whenever the deposit is lost, regardless of fault.
B) Only if he causes loss (ghall) or transgresses (ta'addi).
C) Only if the deposit is stolen from his custody.
D) He is never liable, even if negligent.

Key Takeaway
A deposit is a trust; the depositary is not liable for loss unless he commits treachery or negligence.

Delegation of Deposit to Household Members
Permissibility of delegated custody.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a depositary may delegate custody of a deposit to his household members and explain the two-fold rationale (customary care and necessity).

Core Idea
A depositary may delegate a deposit to his household because he is expected to treat it like his own property.

The Hanafi school permits a depositary (muda') to keep a deposit himself or through his family members, including wife, children, and servants. This lesson unpacks the two rationales cited in the text: the depositary is only obligated to exercise the same level of care he would give his own property, and necessity prevents him from always guarding it personally. Understanding this ruling clarifies how custom ('urf) and implicit consent operate in Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the depositary keeping the deposit either personally or through his household. The ruling is that this is permissible (ja'iz) and does not require the owner's explicit permission.
The term 'iyal (household) includes family and dependents, not strangers.
2
Analyze the first rationale (customary care)
The text argues: 'apparently he undertook to preserve another's property in the same manner as he preserves his own property.' This means the depositary is only expected to exercise the same care he uses for his own belongings, which naturally includes delegating to his household.
This is based on 'urf (custom) and default expectations in the contract.
3
Analyze the second rationale (necessity)
The depositary cannot stay home all the time nor carry the deposit everywhere. Necessity dictates that he may entrust it to his family. The law deems the owner implicitly consenting because no reasonable owner would require constant personal custody.
Necessity (darurah) is a secondary justification reinforcing the first.
4
Define the scope of 'household'
The permission is limited to those in the depositary's 'iyal – his dependents (wife, children, servants). It does not extend to strangers or non-household members without explicit permission.
Later Hanafi scholars refine this: delegation to anyone the depositary would trust with his own property is covered.
5
Connect to implicit consent
The owner's consent is implied by custom and necessity. The depositary does not need express authorization because the standard of care is measured against the depositary's own property management.
This differs from the Shafi'i view, which often requires explicit permission for delegation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلِلْمُودَعِ أَنْ يَحْفَظَهَا بِنَفْسِهِ وَبِمَنْ فِي عِيَالِهِ، لِأَنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أَنَّهُ الْتَزَمَ حِفْظَ مَالِ غَيْرِهِ عَلَى الْوَجْهِ الَّذِي يَحْفَظُ مَالَ نَفْسِهِ، وَلِأَنَّهُ لَا يَجِدُ بُدًّا مِنَ الدَّفْعِ إِلَى عِيَالِهِ، لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُمَكِّنُهُ مُلَازَمَةُ بَيْتِهِ وَلَا اسْتِصْحَابُ الْوَدِيعَةِ فِي خُرُوجِهِ، فَكَانَ الْمَالِكُ رَاضِيًا بِهِ.

ترجمہ: اور مودع کو اختیار ہے کہ ودیعت کی حفاظت کرے بذات خود یا بذریعہ اس کے جو اس کے عیال میں ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ اس نے مال غیر کی حفاظت کا التزام اسی طور پر کیا ہے جس طور پر وہ اپنے مال کی حفاظت کرتا ہے اور اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے عیال کو دیئے بغیر کوئی چارہ نہیں پاتا۔
He said: The depositary may keep it himself or by those under his care (family), because apparently he undertook to preserve another's property in the same manner as he preserves his own property, and because he has no alternative but to entrust it to his family, since he cannot always stay at home nor can he always carry the deposit with him when he goes out, so the owner is considered to have consented to that. Translation: And the depositary has the option to protect the deposit himself or through those who are in his household. Because the apparent understanding is that he committed to protect another's property in the same way that he protects his own property, and because he has no alternative but to delegate to his family.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This is another ruling from the same page. The text gives the depositary (muda') permission to keep the deposit either personally or through his family members (including servants and dependents). The reasoning (ta'lil) is twofold: first, the depositary is only expected to exercise the same level of care over the deposit as over his own property, and he would normally delegate custody to his household for his own belongings. Second, realistically he cannot stay home all the time or carry the deposit everywhere, so necessity dictates that he may entrust it to his family. The law deems the owner's implicit consent since this is customary. This ruling is based on 'urf (custom) and necessity.

Key Terms
الْعِيَالُ (al-'iyal): One's dependents/family members, including wife, children, and household servants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a depositary (muda') give the deposit to his wife to keep?
A) No, he must keep it himself at all times.
B) Yes, because his wife is part of his 'iyal (household).
C) Only if the owner explicitly permits it.
D) He can give it to anyone, even strangers.

Key Takeaway
A depositary may delegate a deposit to his household members without explicit owner permission, based on the principles of customary care and necessity.
