Depository May Keep Deposit With His Household
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute regarding custody by household.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a depository keeping a deposit with his household, identify the proof, and apply the condition that household members must be trustworthy.

Core Idea
A depository may use his household for custody; liability only arises if household members are untrustworthy.

This lesson examines a practical question in the law of deposits (wadīʿah): Can the depository leave the deposit with his family without explicit permission? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on the Prophet's practice and the principle of implicit consent. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the condition that protects the depository from liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the depository leaving the deposit with his household. Identify the hukm: is it permissible or prohibited? Note the Hanafi position.
The ruling is ja'iz (permissible) with a condition.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text for the Hanafi position. What is the source and how does it support the ruling?
The proof is a hadith about the Prophet leaving deposits with Umm Ayman.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith demonstrates permissibility. Which words or actions show that using a household member is allowed?
The Prophet entrusted a deposit to a household member, implying permission.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Identify the opposing view (Shafi'i and Maliki) and their argument. Then examine the Hanafi response.
The opponents argue that entrusting to anyone else is a violation; the Hanafis respond that the owner implicitly consents.
5
Apply the condition
Note the condition mentioned in the text: the household members must be trustworthy (amin). Determine the consequence if they are not.
If the household is not amin, the depository may be liable for loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مودع ودیعت کی خود اور اپنے عیال سے حفاظت کر اسکتا ہے قَالَ وَلِلْمُوْدَعِ أَنْ يَحْفَظَهَا بِنَفْسِهِ وَبِمَنْ فِى عَمَالِهِ لِاَنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أَنَّهُ يَلْتَزِمُ حِفْظُ مَالِ غَيْرِهِ عَلَى الْوَجْهِ الَّذِي يَحْفَظُ مَالَ نَفْسِهِ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَا يَجِدُ بُدَّامِنَ الدَّفع إلى عَيَالِهِ لأَنَّهُ لَا يُمَكِّنُهُ مُلَازَمَةَ بَيْتِهِ وَلَا اسْتَصْحَابَ الْوَدِيعَةِ فِي خُرُوجِهِ فَكَانَ الْمَالِكِ رَاضِيا به ترجمہ اور مودع کو اختیار ہے کہ ودیعت کی حفاظت کرے بذات خود یا بذریعہ اس کے جو اس کے عیال میں ہے۔ کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ اس نے مال غیر کی حفاظت کا التزام اسی طور پر کیا ہے جس طور پر وہ اپنے مال کی حفاظت کرتا ہے اور اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے عیال کو دیئے بغیر کوئی چارہ نہیں پاتا۔ کیونکہ اس کو ہردم اپنی کوٹھری میں رہنا ممکن نہیں۔ اور نہ باہر جانے میں ہر وقت ودیعت کو ساتھ رکھنا ممکن ہے تو مالک ایسی حفاظت۔ ہے۔ مالک الوديعة ں ہو چکا۔ تشریح قوله وللمودع - مودع مال ودیعت کی حفاظت خود کرے یا اپنے اہل وعیال سے کرائے ہیں اور bestus احمد بھی اس کے قائل ہیں۔ امام شافعی اور اشہب مالکی کے نزدیک اہل وعیال کے پاس چھوڑنا جائہ نہیں ، اگر تاز ہو گئی تو ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مالک نے وہ چیز صرف مودہ کی حفاظت میں دی ہے۔ ہماری دلیل وہ روایت ہے جو ابن اسحاق نے بسند قوی : کمر کی ہے۔ انه عليه السلام كانت عنده ودائع فلما اراد الهجرا ودعها عندام ايمن وامر عليا ان يردها على . اهلها قَالَ فاقام على بن ابى طالب خمس ليال وايامها حتى ادّى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الودائع التي كانت عنده للناس علاوہ ازیں مودع ( مالک ودیعت ) کو یہ بات خود بھی معلوم ہے کہ میں جس کے پاس ودیعت رکھ رہا ہوں وہ ودیعت کی وجہ سے نہ ہر وقت گھر میں بیٹھ سکتا ہے اور نہ ودیعت کو ہر جگہ ساتھ لئے پھر سکتا ہے لامحالہ وہ اپنے گھر والوں کے پاس چھوڑے گا اس کے باوجود جب اس نے ودیعت اس کے پاس رکھی ہے تو وہ خود ہی اس سے راضی ہو چکا۔ قوله في عياله... عیال سے مراد وہ لوگ ہیں جو اس کے ساتھ رہتے ہوں حقیقتہ یا حکما یعنی خواہ نان و نفقہ میں شریک ہوں یا نہ ہوں۔ جیسے بیوی، اولاد، والدین، اجیر خاص، اپنا غلام، باندی وغیرہ ( مگر زوجہ اور ولد صغیر میں مساکنت حقیقی ضروری نہیں ) پس جو اجنبی اس کے ساتھ رہتے ہوں ان کے پاس ودیعت رکھنے سے ضمان لازم نہ ہوگا۔ پھر ذخیرہ میں ہے کہ اہل و عیال کے پاس ودیعت چھوڑنے کا جواز اس وقت ہے جب وہ امین ہو۔ ورنہ جائز نہیں۔ فتاؤ کی ابواللیث میں بھی ایسا ہی ہے۔ خزانہ الفقہ میں ہے۔ لاضمان على المودع الافى ثلثة اشياء التقصير في حفظها وخلطها بماله ومنعها عن مالكها بعد الطلب.
The depository may keep the deposit himself or with those in his household... (translation of Arabic) ... and the commentary explains that 'ayal includes wife, children, parents, etc. And in al-Dhakhirah: permissibility is conditioned on them being trustworthy.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position is that the depository (mūda') may keep the deposit either personally or with his household (ʻiyāl). The rationale is twofold: (1) it is presumed that the depository will safeguard the property in the same manner he safeguards his own, which includes using his household; (2) practically, he cannot remain home at all times or carry the deposit everywhere, so he must entrust it to his family. This is supported by a strong hadith from Ibn Ishaq: the Prophet (peace be upon him) had deposits, and when he migrated, he left them with Umm Ayman and ordered 'Ali to return them. The text then notes that Imam Shafi'i and Ashhab al-Maliki disagree—they consider it impermissible to leave the deposit with one's family; if it is destroyed, the depository is liable. The Hanafi response: the owner implicitly consents to this practice by the nature of the arrangement. The commentary further defines 'iyāl: those who live with the depository, whether financially dependent or not (e.g., wife, children, parents, hired servant, slave). Strangers living with him are not included—giving the deposit to them would incur liability. Additionally, from al-Dhakhirah: the permissibility is conditional on the household members being trustworthy (amin). The text concludes with a summary from Khazanat al-Fiqh: the depository is not liable except in three cases: negligence in guarding, mixing the deposit with his own property, and refusing to return it upon demand.

Key Terms
عيال (ʻiyāl): Members of the depository's household who live with him, including wife, children, parents, servants, etc.
أمين (amin): Trustworthy; a person who is reliable for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a depository become liable for leaving the deposit with his household?
A) Always, because the deposit must be kept personally
B) Only if the household member is not trustworthy (not amin)
C) Only if the deposit is destroyed
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The depository may use his household for custody, provided they are trustworthy; otherwise, he bears liability.

Liability of the Depository for Misconduct (Ta'addi)
Stating conditions that make the depository liable for the deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depository becomes liable for the deposit and apply the exceptions to these conditions.

Core Idea
A depository becomes liable if he hands the deposit to a stranger, refuses to return, or fails to prove emergency.

In the law of wadi'ah (custody), the depository is a trusted custodian. However, certain actions transform this trust into liability. This passage from al-Hidayah defines when the depository becomes a guarantor (damin) and when he is excused, with rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary rule triggering liability
Find the action that makes the depository liable: keeping the deposit with a non-household or depositing it with a third party. The reasoning: the owner consented to the depository's hand, not another's.
The term ʻiyāl (household) is significant; the depository's family are considered an extension of himself.
2
Understand the logical proof (qiyas)
The passage analogizes to an agent (wakeel) who cannot delegate without permission. Just as an agent's authority is personal, the depository's trust is personal and cannot be transferred to another hand without the owner's consent.
This is a qiyas jaliy because the illa (reason) is the personal nature of the trust.
3
Recognize the exception for emergencies
In cases of fire or sinking ship, the depository may hand the deposit to a neighbor or another vessel without liability. Necessity and implied consent override the general rule.
Also note the exception for renting a safe: it becomes the depository's own hirz.
4
Note the evidentiary burden on the depository
The depository's claim of emergency is not accepted without clear evidence (bayyinah). He is claiming a necessity that removes liability after the trust was established, similar to claiming permission to entrust.
This shifts the burden of proof to the depository.
5
Apply the rule for refusal to return
If the owner demands the deposit and the depository, being able to deliver, refuses, he becomes liable from the moment of refusal. The detention is an act of transgression (ta'addi) because the owner's demand withdraws consent.
Liability arises even if the deposit is later returned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اپنے عیال کے علاوہ غیر کے پاس ودیعت رکھنے سے ضامن ہوگا فَإِنْ حَفِظَهَا بِغَيْرِهِمْ أَوْ أَوْدَعَهَا غَيْرَهُمْ ضَمِنَ لِاَنَّ الْمَالِكَ رَضِيَ بِيَدِهِ لَا بِيَدِ غَيْرِهِ وَالْأَيْدِي تَخْتَلِفُ فِي الْأَمَانَةِ وَلاَنَّ الشَّيْءَ لَا يَتَضَمَّنَ مِثْلَهُ كَالْوَكِيْل لَا يُوْكَّل غَيْرَه وَالْوَضْعُ فِي حِرْز غَيْرِهِ إِبْدَاعِ إِلَّا إِذَا اسْتَاجَرَ الْحِرْزَ فَيَكُونُ حَافِظًا بحرز نَفْسِهِ. قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَقَعَ فِي دَارِهِ حَريقٌ فَيُسَلِّمُهَا إِلى جَارِهِ أَوْ يَكُونُ فِي سَفِينَةٍ فَخَافَ الْغَرْقَ فَيُلْقِيهَا إِلَى سَفِينَةٍ أُخْرَى لِأَنَّهُ تَعَيَّنَ طَرِيقًا لِلْحِفْظِ فِي هَذِهِ الْحَالَةِ فَيَرْتَضِيْهِ الْمَالِكُ وَلَا يُصَدَّقُ عَلَى ذَالِكَ إِلَّا بِبَيِّنَةٍ لِأَنَّهُ يَدَّعِى ضَرُورَةَ مُسْقِطَةٌ لِلصَّمَانِ بَعْدَ تَحَققِ السَّبَبِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا ادَّعَى الْإِذْنَ فِي الْإِبْدَاعِ. قَالَ فَإِنْ طَلَبَهَا صَاحِبُهَا فَمَنَعَهَا وَهُوَ يَقْدِرُ عَلَى تَسْلِيمِهَا ضَمِنَهَا لِأَنَّهُ مُتَعَدٍ بِالْمَنْعِ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّهُ لَمَا طَالَبَه لَمْ يَكُنْ رَاضِبًا بِإِمْسَاكِهِ بَعْدَهُ فَيَضْمَنُهَا بِحَبْسِهِ عَنْهُ
If he keeps it with others (non-household) or deposits it with them, he is liable, because the owner consented to his hand, not the hand of another, and hands differ in trustworthiness, and a thing does not guarantee another like itself, just as an agent cannot appoint another agent. Placing it in another's safe is entrusting to another except if he rents the safe, then he becomes a guardian via his own safe. He said: except if a fire occurs in his house and he hands it to his neighbor, or if he is on a ship and fears sinking and throws it to another ship, because the only way to preserve it in that situation is determined, so the owner consents. But he is not believed in that without evidence, because he claims a necessity that removes liability after the cause arose, similar to claiming permission to entrust. He said: If the owner demands it and he prevents it while able to deliver, he is liable because he is transgressing by prevention, and because once demanded, the owner is not consenting to its retention, so he becomes liable by detaining it.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with rational proof. It outlines two scenarios where the depository becomes liable:
1. If he keeps the deposit with a third party who is not from his household (ʻiyāl), or if he deposits it with another person, he becomes liable (ḍāmin) for any subsequent loss. The rationale: the owner consented to the depository's trustworthiness specifically, not to someone else's; hands differ in trustworthiness, and a person's trustworthiness does not automatically extend to others. This is analogized to a wakeel (agent) who cannot delegate his authority to another without permission. An exception: if the depository rents a safe (ḥirz) from someone else, that is considered his own safe, so no liability.
2. An exception to the above: if a sudden emergency occurs, such as a fire in his house requiring him to hand the deposit to a neighbor, or if he is on a sinking ship and must transfer it to another ship, he does not become liable because in such dire circumstances, preserving the deposit takes priority and the owner would implicitly consent. However, the depository's claim of such an emergency is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah), because he is asserting a necessity that removes liability after the cause (the trust) has been established. This is similar to claiming permission to entrust the deposit.
3. If the owner demands the return of the deposit and the depository, being able to do so, refuses and detains it, he becomes liable from the moment of refusal because his detention is an act of transgression (ta'addi). The owner's demand implies withdrawal of consent to keep the deposit.

Key Terms
تعدّي (ta'addi): Transgression or misconduct that transforms the trust (amanah) into liability (daman).
حرز (hirz): A secure place for safekeeping property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does the depository NOT become liable for the deposit?
A) He keeps it with a neighbor during a fire in his house
B) He deposits it with a stranger without emergency
C) He refuses to return it upon demand while able
D) He rents a safe from another and places it there

Key Takeaway
A depository becomes liable when he deviates from proper custody by entrusting to a stranger or refusing to return, unless an emergency is proven.

Deposit Mixing and Liability: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Partnership
Intra-Hanafi ruling on mixing deposit with own property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mixing deposit with one's own property, explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each view.

Core Idea
Mixing deposit with your own property makes you liable; but can the depositor claim a share? Abu Hanifa says no; Sahibayn says yes if same kind.

This passage from the Hidayah deals with a common scenario: a depository accidentally mixes the deposit with his own property so that they become indistinguishable. The text establishes the liability, but then reveals a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school about the depositor's recourse. Understanding this difference is crucial for applying fiqh to modern storage and commingling cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger and its consequence
Mixing the deposit with the depository's own property until indistinguishable triggers liability (daman). This is uncontested.
The key phrase: "حَتَّى لَا يَتَمَيَّزُ ضَمِنَهَا" – 'such that it cannot be distinguished, he becomes liable.'
2
Contrast the two positions on recourse
Abu Hanifa: depositor has no specific claim on the mixed property; it becomes a debt. Sahibayn: if mixed with same kind, depositor may choose partnership.
The dispute appears in the words: "ثُمَّ لَا سَبِيلَ لِلْمُودِعِ عَلَيْهَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةٌ وَقَالَا ... شِرْكَهُ إِنْ شَاءَ"
3
Understand the reasoning (taʿlīl)
Abu Hanifa sees mixing as destroying the property's identity, so liability replaces the original. Sahibayn see mixing with same kind as creating co-ownership, by analogy to cases where two persons pool funds.
The Sahibayn's view relies on qiyas and istihsan – a more flexible approach.
4
Extract the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the mixed property is sold, Abu Hanifa gives the depositor the price as a debt; Sahibayn give him a share of the mixed property as co-owner.
This affects whether the depositor benefits from market appreciation of the commodity.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g. al-Hidayah) often prefer the Sahibayn's view for practicality and justice.
This is not stated in the passage but is the established tarjih in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ خَلَطَهَا الْمُوْدَعُ بِمَالِهِ حَتَّى لَا يَتَمَيَّزُ ضَمِنَهَا ثُمَّ لَا سَبِيلَ لِلْمُودِعِ عَلَيْهَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةٌ وَقَالَا إِذَا خَلَطَهَا بِجِنْسِهَا شِرْكَهُ إِنْ شَاءَ مِثْلُ أَنْ يَخْلِطَ الدَّرَاهِمَ الْبَيْضَ بِالْبِيْضِ وَالسُّودَ بِالسُّودِ وَالْحِنْطَةَ بِالْحِنْطَةِ وَالشَّعِيرَ بِالشَّعِيرِ
He said: If the depository mixes it with his own property such that it cannot be distinguished, he becomes liable for it. Then, according to Abu Hanifa, the depositor has no recourse to the property; but the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he mixes it with something of the same kind, it becomes a partnership if the depositor wishes, such as mixing white dirhams with white, black with black, wheat with wheat, barley with barley.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the consequence of mixing the deposit with the depository's own property. The ruling: if the depository mixes the deposit with his own property in such a way that it becomes indistinguishable (lā yatamayyaz), he becomes liable (ḍāmin) for the deposit. This is because his mixing constitutes transgression (ta'addi), converting the trust into a guaranteed property. The dispute is about the depositor's recourse after liability: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the depositor has no specific claim on the mixed property; rather, the depository must compensate the depositor with an equivalent (mithl) or value (qīmah) if it is a genus that cannot be precisely identified. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold a different view: if the mixing is with property of the same kind (jins), such as mixing silver dirhams with silver dirhams of the same type, or wheat with wheat, then the depositor may choose to take a share of the mixed property as a partner (shirkah). The mixing creates a joint ownership (shirkat al-māl) according to their view. The text gives examples: white dirhams with white, black dirhams with black, wheat with wheat, barley with barley. The dispute turns on whether the depositor can reclaim his property in kind once it is mixed; Abu Hanifa sees impossibility of separation leading to full liability, while the Sahibayn see potential for co-ownership. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is often the view of the Sahibayn in such cases due to practice, but the text does not explicitly give tarjih here.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposit with the depository's own property so that it becomes indistinguishable.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership; here, co-ownership of the mixed property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, what happens if the depository mixes wheat deposit with his own wheat indistinguishably?
A) The depository is liable only for the value, and the depositor loses claim to the wheat
B) The depositor may become a partner in the mixed wheat if he wishes
C) The mixing is permissible and no liability arises
D) The depository must immediately compensate with other wheat

Key Takeaway
Mixing deposit with same kind creates liability, but the Sahibayn allow the depositor to claim a share as partner, which is the preferred view for modern practice.

Depositary May Entrust Deposit to His Household: Hanafi Position and Proof
Explaining that depositary may deposit with family based on prophetic practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a depositary entrusting the deposit to his household, cite the primary proof (dalil naqli), and distinguish the Hanafi position from that of Imam al-Shafi'i and Ashhab al-Maliki.

Core Idea
The depositary may entrust the deposit to his household; the Prophet ﷺ did so, and the owner implicitly consents.

This lesson examines a key point of difference between the Hanafi school and other madhahib regarding the duties of a depositary (al-mudi'). The question is whether the depositary may delegate the physical custody of the deposit to his household members. The Hanafi school, following the practice of the Prophet ﷺ, permits this, while Imam al-Shafi'i and Ashhab (Maliki) hold it as a trespass. We will explore the proof and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: it is permissible for the depositary to entrust the deposit to his household (ahl wa 'iyal). If the deposit is lost while with them, the depositary is not liable provided they are trustworthy.
This permissibility is based on prophetic practice and necessity.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i and Ashhab al-Maliki hold that entrusting to others is a trespass (ta'addi). If the deposit is lost while with the household, the depositary is liable for the loss.
Their view is strict: the owner's explicit trust is in the depositary alone.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opposing dalil: the owner gave the deposit specifically to the depositary for safekeeping; transferring custody to others without permission violates this trust.
This is a logical argument based on contractual intent.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response cites the Prophet's action: he left deposits with Umm Ayman and ordered Ali to return them. Additionally, custom dictates that the owner implicitly consents because he knows the depositary cannot always keep the deposit physically with him.
The Hanafi school also adds the condition of trustworthiness and necessity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If the deposit is lost while with the household, Hanafis do not impose liability (subject to trustworthiness), while Shafi'is and Ashhab impose liability unless the depositary proves an emergency.
This difference affects the rights and responsibilities in deposit contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح قوله وللمودع - مودع مال ودیعت کی حفاظت خود کرے یا اپنے اہل وعیال سے کرائے ہیں اور احمد بھی اس کے قائل ہیں۔ امام شافعی اور اشہب مالکی کے نزدیک اہل وعیال کے پاس چھوڑنا جائہ نہیں ، اگر تاز ہو گئی تو ضامن ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مالک نے وہ چیز صرف مودہ کی حفاظت میں دی ہے۔ ہماری دلیل وہ روایت ہے جو ابن اسحاق نے بسند قوی : کمر کی ہے۔ انه عليه السلام كانت عنده ودائع فلما اراد الهجرا ودعها عندام ايمن وامر عليا ان يردها على . اهلها قَالَ فاقام على بن ابى طالب خمس ليال وايامها حتى ادّى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الودائع التي كانت عنده للناس علاوہ ازیں مودع ( مالک ودیعت ) کو یہ بات خود بھی معلوم ہے کہ میں جس کے پاس ودیعت رکھ رہا ہوں وہ ودیعت کی وجہ سے نہ ہر وقت گھر میں بیٹھ سکتا ہے اور نہ ودیعت کو ہر جگہ ساتھ لئے پھر سکتا ہے لامحالہ وہ اپنے گھر والوں کے پاس چھوڑے گا اس کے باوجود جب اس نے ودیعت اس کے پاس رکھی ہے تو وہ خود ہی اس سے راضی ہو چکا۔ قوله في عياله... عیال سے مراد وہ لوگ ہیں جو اس کے ساتھ رہتے ہوں حقیقتہ یا حکما یعنی خواہ نان و نفقہ میں شریک ہوں یا نہ ہوں۔ جیسے بیوی، اولاد، والدین، اجیر خاص، اپنا غلام، باندی وغیرہ ( مگر زوجہ اور ولد صغیر میں مساکنت حقیقی ضروری نہیں ) پس جو اجنبی اس کے ساتھ رہتے ہوں ان کے پاس ودیعت رکھنے سے ضمان لازم نہ ہوگا۔ پھر ذخیرہ میں ہے کہ اہل و عیال کے پاس ودیعت چھوڑنے کا جواز اس وقت ہے جب وہ امین ہو۔ ورنہ جائز نہیں۔ فتاؤ کی ابواللیث میں بھی ایسا ہی ہے۔ خزانہ الفقہ میں ہے۔ لاضمان على المودع الافى ثلثة اشياء التقصير في حفظها وخلطها بماله ومنعها عن مالكها بعد الطلب. اپنے عیال کے علاوہ غیر کے پاس ودیعت رکھنے سے ضامن ہوگا
فيه شئ و هو انه يشعر بكون مدار جواز دفع الوديعة الى عياله رضا المالك به و ذالك يقتضى عدم جواز دفعها اليه عند عدم رضاه و لیس كذالك فان المالك اذا نهى عن دفعها الى احد من عياله فدفعها الى من لا بد له منه لم يضمن كما سياتى فالظاهر ان مدار ذالك هو الضرورة فالاولى ان يترك فكان المالك و راضیا به
Explanation of his statement 'and for the depositary' - The depositary may protect the deposited property himself or have his household protect it. Ahmad also holds this view. According to Imam Shafi'i and Ashhab al-Maliki, leaving it with household is not permissible; if it is lost, he will be liable. Because the owner gave it only for the depositary's protection. Our proof is the narration from Ibn Ishaq with a strong chain: The Prophet (peace be upon him) had deposits with him. When he intended to emigrate, he left them with Umm Ayman and ordered Ali to return them to their owners. Ali stayed five nights and days until he delivered the deposits that were with the Prophet (peace be upon him) to the people. Additionally, the owner (of the deposit) himself knows that the person with whom he is depositing cannot stay home all the time because of the deposit, nor can he carry it everywhere. Inevitably, he will leave it with his family. Despite that, when he placed the deposit with him, he has consented to this. His statement 'in his household'... The meaning of 'iyal' are those who live with him, either actually or legally, whether they share in food and maintenance or not. Like wife, children, parents, specific hired servant, one's own slave, slave-girl etc. (But for wife and minor child, actual cohabitation is not necessary). So if he leaves the deposit with a stranger who lives with him, he will not be liable. Then in al-Dhakhirah: The permissibility of leaving the deposit with household is when they are trustworthy, otherwise not. It is similarly in Fatawa Abi al-Layth. In Khazanat al-Fiqh: The depositary is not liable except in three things: negligence in its preservation, mixing it with his own property, and preventing it from its owner after demand. Leaving the deposit with someone other than one's household makes him liable. There is a point: This indicates that the basis for permissibility of giving the deposit to his household is the owner's consent. This would imply that it is not permissible when the owner does not consent. But that is not the case, because if the owner forbids giving it to any of his household, and he gives it to someone he cannot avoid, he will not be liable, as will come. So the apparent basis is necessity, so it is better to leave it, as if the owner is pleased.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the depositary entrusting the deposit to his household. The Hanafi position, shared by Imam Ahmad, is that it is permissible. The proof is the practice of the Prophet ﷺ who left deposits with Umm Ayman and ordered Ali to return them. Additionally, logic dictates consent: the owner knows the depositary cannot stay home always. The definition of 'iyal includes those living with the depositary, even if not dependents. A later note clarifies that the true basis is necessity, not merely consent, since even if the owner forbids, the depositary is not liable if necessity dictates. The passage ends with a summary from Khazanat al-Fiqh listing the three cases of liability.

Key Terms
عیال ('iyal): Household members of the depositary, including wife, children, parents, servants, and slaves living with him, whether or not he supports them financially.
وديعة (wadi'ah): A contract of deposit where one party entrusts property to another for safekeeping without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it permissible for the depositary to entrust the deposit to his household?
A) Because the household is more trustworthy than the depositary
B) Because the owner explicitly permits it in the contract
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) did so, and the owner implicitly consents
D) Because the deposit is not valuable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the depositary to entrust the deposit to his household based on the Prophet's practice and necessity; loss without negligence incurs no liability.

8 min
Liability for Entrusting Deposit to Others and Refusing Return
Statutory ruling with exception and evidence requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two circumstances under which a depositary becomes liable (damān) according to the Hanafi school and identify the exception for emergencies and the proof requirement.

Core Idea
Giving the deposit to a non-household or refusing to return it makes the depositary liable, unless an emergency is proven with evidence.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we examine the boundaries of a depositary's duty. The general rule is strict: the depositary must keep the deposit himself, with his household. If he gives it to someone outside his household, or if he refuses to return it when demanded, he becomes liable for compensation. However, there is a narrow exception for genuine emergencies, but the depositary must prove the necessity with evidence. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rationale, and the evidentiary requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts that trigger liability
From the passage, find the two actions that make the depositary liable: (1) giving the deposit to someone other than his household (non-household), and (2) refusing to return the deposit to the owner when able to do so after demand.
The passage explicitly states 'if he gives it to others' and 'if he refuses when demanded'.
2
Understand the rationale for liability
The author gives reasons: the owner consented only to the depositary's hand, not another's; hands differ in trustworthiness; a deposit does not contain its like, so the depositary cannot delegate (analogy with agency).
These are aqli (rational) proofs listed in the text.
3
Recognize the exception for emergencies
The passage exempts situations of necessity, such as fire in the house or fear of drowning on a ship. In such cases, giving to another is permitted because the means of preservation is determined.
The exemption is conditional: the depositary must prove the emergency with evidence.
4
Note the evidentiary rule
The depositary's claim of necessity is not accepted without proof (baiyinah). He must bring evidence to avoid liability, similar to claiming permission to entrust.
This is a key procedural point: the default is that giving to non-household is a transgression; the burden of proof is on the depositary to show the exception applies.
5
Consider the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi school distinguishes between household and non-household, allowing the depositary to give to his family. The Shafi'i school does not make this distinction and holds that any delegation, even to household, triggers liability.
This difference illustrates how implied consent is interpreted differently across schools.

فَإِنْ حَفِظَهَا بِغَيْرِهِمْ أَوْ أَوْدَعَهَا غَيْرَهُمْ ضَمِنَ لِاَنَّ الْمَالِكَ رَضِيَ بِيَدِهِ لَا بِيَدِ غَيْرِهِ وَالْأَيْدِي تَخْتَلِفُ فِي الْأَمَانَةِ وَلاَنَّ الشَّيْءَ لَا يَتَضَمَّنَ مِثْلَهُ كَالْوَكِيْل لَا يُوْكَّل غَيْرَه وَالْوَضْعُ فِي حِرْز غَيْرِهِ إِبْدَاعِ إِلَّا إِذَا اسْتَاجَرَ الْحِرْزَ فَيَكُونُ حَافِظًا بحرز نَفْسِهِ. قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَقَعَ فِي دَارِهِ حَريقٌ فَيُسَلِّمُهَا إِلى جَارِهِ أَوْ يَكُونُ فِي سَفِينَةٍ فَخَافَ الْغَرْقَ فَيُلْقِيهَا إِلَى سَفِينَةٍ أُخْرَى لِأَنَّهُ تَعَيَّنَ طَرِيقًا لِلْحِفْظِ فِي هَذِهِ الْحَالَةِ فَيَرْتَضِيْهِ الْمَالِكُ وَلَا يُصَدَّقُ عَلَى ذَالِكَ إِلَّا بِبَيِّنَةٍ لِأَنَّهُ يَدَّعِى ضَرُورَةَ مُسْقِطةٌ لِلصَّمَانِ بَعْدَ تَحَققِ السَّبَبِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا ادَّعَى الْإِذْنَ فِي الْإِبْدَاعِ. قَالَ فَإِنْ طَلَبَهَا صَاحِبُهَا فَمَنَعَهَا وَهُوَ يَقْدِرُ عَلَى تَسْلِيمِهَا ضَمِنَهَا لِأَنَّهُ مُتَعَدٍ بِالْمَنْعِ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّهُ لَمَا طَالَبَه لَمْ يَكُنْ رَاضِبًا بِإِمْسَاكِهِ بَعْدَهُ فَيَضْمَنُهَا بِحَبْسِهِ عَنْهُ
ترجمہ: پس اگر ان کے غیر سے حفاظت کرائی یا غیر کے پاس ودیعت رکھ دی تو اس کے قبضہ ہوا۔ اور ہاتھ مختلف ہوتے ہیں امانت میں، اور اس لئے کہ شئی اپنے متضمن نہیں ہوتی اپنے دوسرے کی حرز میں رکھنا بھی ودیعت دینا ہے مگر جب جز کو کرایہ پر لیا ہو کہ اس صورت میں ذاتی حرز سے حفاظت ہوگی۔ لیکن یہ کہ آگ لگ جائے اس کے گھر میں پس وہ دے دے اپنے پڑوسی کو یا وہ کشتی میں ہو اور ڈوبنے کے اندیشہ سے اس کو دوسری کشتی میں ڈال دے کیونکہ اس حالت میں حفاظت کا یہی طریقہ متعین ہو گیا۔ تو مالک اس سے راضی ہو گا اور اس بارے میں تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی مگر بینہ کے ساتھ کیونکہ وہ ایسی ضرورت کا مدعی ہے جو ضمان ساقط کرنے والی ہے۔ تحقق سبب کے بعد تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے ودیعت رکھنے کی اجازت کا دعویٰ کرے۔ پھر اگر مالک نے ودیعت طلب کی اور اس نے روک لی حالانکہ وہ دے سکتا تھا تو وہ ضامن ہوگا کیونکہ وہ روکنے سے متعدی ہو گیا۔ اور یہ اس لئے کہ جب مالک نے ودیعت طلب کرلی تو اس کے بعد وہ اس کے پاس رکھنے سے راضی نہیں رہا۔ پس روکنے سے ضامن ہو جائے گا۔
تشریح: قوله فان حفظها بغير هم اگر مودع نے مال ودیعت اپنے عیال کے سوا کسی اور کی حفاظت میں دیا یا کسی کے پاس ودیعت رکھا تو ضامن ہو گا اس لئے کہ مالک مستودع کے قبضہ سے راضی ہے نہ کہ غیر کے قبضہ سے تو غیر کی حفاظت میں دینا مالک کے رضا کے بغیر ہوا ممکن ہے کوئی یہ کہے کہ جیسے اس کا هاتھ ہے ایسے ہی غیر کا ہاتھ ہے۔ والایـدی تـخـتـلـف اس کا جواب ہے کہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ از راہ امانت لوگوں کے ہاتھ مختلف ہوتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ بعض لوگ امانت کے پورے نگہبان ہوتے ہیں اور بعض خائن۔ قوله و لان الشئى لا يتضمن مستودع غیر کے پاس ودیعت اس لئے بھی نہیں رکھ سکتا کہ مالک نے ودیعت اس کو دی ہے اور شئی اپنے مثل کو متضمن نہیں ہوتی بلکہ کمتر کو تضمن ہوسکتی ہے۔ جیسے وکیل کو یہ اختیار نہین ہوتا کہ وہ غیر کو وکیل کرے اسی طرح مضارب کو یہ اختیار نہیں کہ راس المال کسی دوسرے کو مضاربت پر دے دے۔ ہاں بضاعت وغیرہ دینے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ مضاربت سے کم تر ہے۔ اگر کوئی یہ کہے کہ اس نے ودیعت نہیں دی بلکہ غیر کے حرز (مکان حفاظت) میں رکھ دی۔ والوضع في حرز سے اس کا جواب ہے کہ دوسرے کی حرز میں رکھنا یہی ودیعت دینا ہے تو جیسے صراحی غیر کو ودیعت دینے میں ضامن ہوتا ہے ایسے ہی حرز غیر میں رکھنے سے ضامن ہوگا۔ قوله الا ان يقع - الخ قول سابق "فان حفضها بغير هم ضمن" سے استثناء ہے مطلب یہ ہے کہ غیر عیال کی حفاظت میں دینے سے وجوب ضمان اس وقت ہے جب بقصد و اختیار ہو اگر بحالت اضطرار دیا تو ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ مثلاً اس کے گھر میں آگ لگ گئی اس لئے اس نے اپنے پڑوی کو دے دی یا وہ کشتی میں سوار تھا۔ پس غرقاب ہونے کے اندیشہ سے دوسری کشتی میں ڈال دی تو ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ایسی حالت میں حفاظت کا یہی طریقہ متعین ہے تو مالک یقینا اس سے راضی ہو گا۔ پھر اگر مودن یہ کہے کہ میں نے صدارت مذکور پیش آنے کی وجہ سے ایسا کیا تھا تو یہ بات بینہ کے بغیر نہیں مانی جائے گی اس واسطے کہ اس کی طرف سے غیر کز بینا پنیا گیا جو موجب منمان ہے اور وہ قول مذکور سے سقوط ضمان کا مدعی ہے۔ تو بینہ سے ثابت کرنا ضروری ہوگا۔ جیسہ کی اگر وہ یہ کہے کہ مجھے مودع نے غیر کو دینے کی اجازت دے دی تھی تو یہ بات بعینہ کے بغیر قبول نہیں ہوتی۔
If he protects it with others (non-household) or entrusts it to them, he is liable because the owner consented to his hand, not another's, and hands differ in trustworthiness, and because a thing does not contain its like, just as an agent cannot appoint another agent. And placing it in another's safe place is entrusting it to another, except if he rents the safe place, then he is protecting it with his own safe place. He said: Except if there is a fire in his house, so he hands it to his neighbor, or he is on a ship and fears drowning, so he throws it to another ship, because in this situation a means of preservation is determined, so the owner would consent. And he will not be believed in that except with evidence, because he claims a necessity that removes liability after the cause is established, so it is like when he claims permission for entrusting. He said: If the owner demands it and he prevents it while able to deliver, he is liable for it because he is transgressing by prevention. And this is because when he demanded, he is no longer pleased with his retention after that, so he is liable by his detention of it. Translation: So if he protects with others or entrusts to them, then it became his possession. And hands differ in trustworthiness, and because a thing does not contain its like, placing in another's safe place is also entrusting it to another, except when he rents the safe place, then he protects with his own safe place. But if a fire occurs in his house, so he gives it to his neighbor, or he is on a ship and fears drowning, so he throws it to another ship, because in this situation a means of preservation is determined, so the owner would consent. And he will not be believed except with evidence, because he claims a necessity that removes liability after the cause is established, so it becomes like he claims permission for entrusting. Then if the owner demands the deposit and he prevents it while able to deliver, he is liable because he becomes transgressor by prevention. And this is because when the owner demanded, he is no longer pleased with his retention after that, so he is liable by his detention. Explanation: His statement 'if he protects with others' - If the depositary gives the deposit to someone other than his household for protection or entrusts it to someone, he is liable because the owner is pleased with the depositary's possession, not another's. So giving to another's protection is without the owner's consent. Someone might say: just as his hand is, so is another's hand. The response to that is not so; rather, from the perspective of trustworthiness, people's hands differ. So some people are complete guardians of trust, and some are treacherous. His statement 'and because a thing does not contain its like' - He cannot entrust to another also because the owner gave the deposit to him, and a thing does not contain its like, but can contain something lesser. Just as an agent does not have the authority to appoint another agent, similarly a mudarib does not have the authority to give the capital to another for mudarabah. Yes, he has authority to give bida'ah etc., because it is lesser than mudarabah. If someone says: He did not give the deposit, but placed it in another's safe place. The response from 'placing in a safe place' is that placing in another's safe place is the same as giving the deposit to another. So just as he becomes liable by giving the deposit to another, similarly he becomes liable by placing in another's safe place. His statement 'except if occurs' etc. - It is an exception from the previous statement 'if he protects with others he is liable'. The meaning is that liability for giving to non-household protection is when it is intentional and voluntary. If he gives in a state of necessity, he will not be liable. For example, a fire broke out in his house, so he gives it to his neighbor, or he was on a ship and fears drowning, so he throws it to another ship, then he will not be liable. Because in such a state, this method is determined for preservation, so the owner would certainly consent. Then if the depositary says: I did this because of the mentioned emergency, this statement will not be accepted without evidence. Because he has placed the deposit in another's hand, which is a cause of liability, and he claims the mentioned statement removes liability. So it is necessary to prove it with evidence. Just as if he says: The owner permitted me to give to another, this statement is not accepted without evidence.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings: (1) if the depositary gives the deposit to someone other than his household, he becomes liable, with exceptions for emergencies (fire, shipwreck) where necessity determines the method; and (2) if the owner demands the deposit and the depositary refuses while able, he becomes liable. The reasons include that the owner only consented to the depositary's hand, not another's; hands differ in trustworthiness; and a deposit cannot entail its like (i.e., the depositary cannot delegate). The exception for emergency is only accepted with evidence. This is followed by detailed commentary explaining the reasoning and analogies with agency and mudarabah.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage to the deposit due to transgression (ta'addi).
حرز (ḥirz): A safe place for protection; placing deposit in another's hirz is akin to entrusting to that person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the depositary gives the deposit to a neighbor because of a fire in his house, according to the Hanafi ruling:
A) He is liable unless he proves the fire with evidence
B) He is not liable because it was a necessity
C) He is liable regardless because giving to non-household always incurs liability
D) He is not liable only if the owner previously permitted it

Key Takeaway
A depositary becomes liable for deposit if he gives it to non-household or refuses return, unless an emergency is proven with evidence.

Deposit Mixed with Own Property: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on mixing deposit with own property and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the liability for mixing a deposit with the depositary's own property of the same genus, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit with identical property: Abu Hanifa gives only compensation; the Sahibayn give the owner a choice to become a partner.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: what happens when a depositary (mustawda') mixes the deposit (wadi'ah) with his own property of the same genus so that the two are indistinguishable. The ruling affects whether the owner can claim a share in the mixture or only compensation. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to see how different usul lead to different furu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is mixing the deposit with depositary's own property of the same genus until indistinguishable. The dispute is about the owner's right: can he claim a specific share or only compensation?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its legal effect
Abu Hanifa rules that the owner has no claim on the mixed property (lā sabīl), only a claim for compensation (damān). The depositary becomes liable for the value.
He views the mixing as ta'addi (wrongful possession) that prevents specific identification.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and the option given to the owner
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say: if mixed with same genus, the owner can choose to become a partner (shirkah) proportionally in the mixture. He may instead claim compensation if he wishes.
They consider the mixture analogous to a debt where the owner can claim a share.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the value of the property fluctuates, the owner might prefer a share (Sahibayn) rather than fixed compensation (Abu Hanifa). The mufta bihi is often the Sahibayn's view.
Later Hanafi practice leans toward the Sahibayn for flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ خَلَطَهَا الْمُوْدَعُ بِمَالِهِ حَتَّى لَا يَتَمَيَّزُ ضَمِنَهَا ثُمَّ لَا سَبِيلَ لِلْمُودِعِ عَلَيْهَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةٌ وَقَالَا إِذَا خَلَطَهَا بِجِنْسِهَا شِرْكَهُ إِنْ شَاءَ مِثْلُ أَنْ يَخْلِطَ الدَّرَاهِمَ الْبَيْضَ بِالْبِيْضِ وَالسُّودَ بِالسُّودِ وَالْحِنْطَةَ بِالْحِنْطَةِ وَالشَّعِيرَ بِالشَّعِيرِ
ترجمہ: اگر ملالیا مودع نے ودیعت کو اپنے مال میں اس طرح امتیاز نہیں ہوسکتا تو ضامن ہوگا پھر مودع کے لئے کوئی راہ نہیں۔ ودیعت پر امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر ودیعت کو اسی کی جنس میں ملایا تو اس میں شریک ہو جائے۔ اگر چاہے۔ مثلاً دودھیا دراہم کو دودھیا درہموں کے ساتھ اور سیاہ درا ہم کو سیاہ درہموں کے ساتھ یا گیہوں کو گیہوں کے ساتھ یادو کو جو کے ساتھ ملا دیا۔
And if the depositary mixes it with his property until it becomes indistinguishable, he becomes liable for it. Then there is no way for the owner to take it (from him) according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: if he mixes it with its own genus, then he becomes a partner if he wishes. For example, he mixes white dirhams with white dirhams, black dirhams with black dirhams, wheat with wheat, barley with barley. Translation: If the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property in such a way that it cannot be distinguished, then he is liable. Then there is no recourse for the owner (to claim the specific property) according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Sahibayn) say: if he mixes it with its own genus, then he becomes a partner if he wishes. For example, white dirhams with white dirhams, black dirhams with black dirhams, wheat with wheat, barley with barley.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage contains an intra-Hanafi dispute about mixing the deposit with the depositary's property. If the deposit is mixed so that it cannot be distinguished from the depositary's own property of the same genus, the depositary becomes liable (he owes the value). Abu Hanifa holds that the owner cannot claim ownership of any part of the mixture; he only has a claim for the value. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the owner can choose to become a partner in the mixture proportionally. Examples include mixing silver dirhams of the same type, or wheat with wheat. The Arabic matn is from Al-Hidayah, and the Urdu translation follows.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposit with the depositary's own property so that it cannot be distinguished.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership in the mixed property; the owner becomes a co-owner proportionally if he chooses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), if the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property of the same genus so that it cannot be distinguished, what option does the owner have?
A) He can only demand compensation for the value
B) He can choose to become a partner in the mixture proportionally
C) He can take all of the mixture
D) He cannot claim anything because he consented to the mixing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a deposit is mixed indistinguishably with the depositary's own property of the same genus, the owner can either claim compensation (Abu Hanifa) or opt for a proportional share (Sahibayn, the mufta bihi).

Depositary's Liability for Entrusting to a Third Party: The Ruling and Its Proofs
Ruling with rational proofs from Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a depositary becomes liable when he gives the deposit to a non-family member, and explain the three rational proofs (dalil aqli) used in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Giving the deposit to a stranger makes the depositary liable because the owner only trusted him, and trustworthiness varies; a thing does not imply its like.

In this passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya, the author establishes the basic rule of liability for a depositary who entrusts the deposit to someone outside his family. He then provides three rational proofs to counter potential objections. Understanding these proofs is essential to grasp the Hanafi position on the limits of a bailee's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its consequence
The depositary gives the deposit to a non-family member or places it with another person. The consequence is that he becomes liable (dāmin) for the deposit.
The rule applies to both active giving and constructive giving (placing in another's safe).
2
First proof: hands differ in trust
The objection that another's hand is like his own is refuted by noting that people's hands (possession) differ in trustworthiness; some are faithful, others treacherous. Thus, the owner's consent to the bailee does not extend to others.
This is a rational observation based on experience.
3
Second proof: a thing does not contain its like
The deposit is a specific thing given to the bailee; the bailee cannot delegate because a thing does not imply its equal. Only a lesser authority (like bidā'ah) can be delegated, but not the deposit itself.
Analogous to the rule that an agent cannot appoint another agent without permission.
4
Third proof: placing in another's safe is giving the deposit
If the depositary argues he merely placed the deposit in another's safe (hirz) without handing over, the answer is that placing in another's safe is legally equivalent to giving possession, so liability follows.
This closes a potential loophole.

كتاب الوديعة . 194 ترجمہ پس اگر ان کے غیر سے حفاظت کرائی یا غیر کے پاس ودیعت رکھ دی تو کے قبضہ ہوا۔ اور ہاتھ مختلف ہوتے ہیں۔ امانت میں اور اس لئے کہ شئی اپنے متضمن نہیں ہوتی اپنے دوسرے کی حرز میں رکھنا بھی ودیعت دینا ہے مگر جب جز کو کرایہ پر لیا ہو کہ اس صورت میں ذاتی حرز سے حفاظت نہ ہوگا۔ لا یہ کہ آگ لگ جائے اس کے گھر میں پس وہ دے دے اپنے پڑوسی کو یا وہ کشتی میں ہو اور ڈوبنے کے اندیشہ سے اس کو دوسری کشتی میں ڈال دے کیونکہ اس حالت میں حفاظت کا یہی طریقہ متعین ہو گیا۔ تو مالک اس سے راضی ہو گا اور اس بارے میں تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی۔ مگر بینہ کے ساتھ کیونکہ وہ ایسی ضرورت کا مدعی ہے جو ضمان ساقط کرنے والی ہے۔ تحقق سبب کے بعد تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے ودیعت رکھنے کی اجازت کا دعویٰ کرے۔ پھر اگر مالک نے ودیعت طلب کی اور اس نے روک لی حالانکہ وہ دے سکتا تھا تو وہ ضامن ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ وہ روکنے سے متعدی ہو گیا۔ اور یہ اس لئے کہ جب مالک نے ودیعت طلب کرلی تو اس کے بعد وہ اس کے پاس رکھنے سے راضی نہیں رہا۔ پس روکنے سے ضامن ہو جائے گا۔ تشریح... قوله فان حفظها بغير هم اگر مودع نے مال ودیعت اپنے عیال کے سوا کسی اور کی حفاظت میں دیا یا کسی کے پاس ودیعت رکھا تو ضامن ہو گا اس لئے کہ مالک مستودع کے قبضہ سے راضی ہے نہ کہ غیر کے قبضہ سے تو غیر کی حفاظت میں دینا مالک کے رضا کے بغیر ہوا ممکن ہے کوئی یہ کہے کہ جیسے اس کا ہاتھ ہے ایسے ہی غیر کا ہاتھ ہے۔ والأیدی تختلف اہ سے اس کا جواب ہے کہ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ از راہ امانت لوگوں کے ہاتھ مختلف ہوتے ہیں۔ چنانچہ بعض لوگ امانت کے پورے نگہبان ہوتے ہیں اور بعض خائن۔ قوله و لان الشئى لا يتضمن مستودع غیر کے پاس ودیعت اس لئے بھی نہیں رکھ سکتا کہ مالک نے ودیعت اس کو دی ہے اور شئی اپنے مثل کو متضمن نہیں ہوتی بلکہ کمتر کو تضمن ہوسکتی ہے۔ جیسے وکیل کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہوتا کہ وہ غیر کو وکیل کرے اسی طرح مضارب کو یہ اختیار نہیں کہ راس المال کسی دوسرے کو مضاربت پر دے دے۔ ہاں بضاعت وغیرہ دینے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ مضاربت سے کم تر ہے۔ اگر کوئی یہ کہے کہ اس نے ودیعت نہیں دی بلکہ غیر کے حرز ( مکان حفاظت ) میں رکھ دی۔ والوضع في حرز سے اس کا جواب ہے کہ دوسرے کی حرز میں رکھنا یہی ودیعت دینا ہے تو جیسے صراحی غیر کو ودیعت دینے میں ضامن ہوتا ہے ایسے ہی حرز غیر میں رکھنے سے ضامن ہوگا۔ قوله الا ان يقع - الخ قول سابق " فان حفضها بغير هم ضمن" سے استثناء ہے مطلب یہ ہے کہ غیر عیال کی حفاظت میں دینے سے وجوب ضمان اس وقت ہے جب بقصد و اختیار ہو اگر بحالت اضطرار دیا تو ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ مثلاً اس کے گھر میں آگ لگ گئی اس لئے اس نے اپنے پڑوسی کو دے دی یا وہ کشتی میں سوار تھا۔ پس غرقاب ہونے کے اندیشہ سے دوسری کشتی میں ڈال دی تو ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ایسی حالت میں حفاظت کا یہی طریقہ متعین ہے تو مالک یقینا اس سے راضی ہو گا۔ پھر اگر مودع یہ کہے کہ میں نے ضرورت مذکور پیش آنے کی وجہ سے ایسا کیا تھا تو یہ بات بینہ کے بغیر نہیں مانی جائے گی اس واسطے کہ اس کی طرف سے غیر کو قبضہ دیا گیا جو موجب ضمان ہے اور وہ قول مذکور سے سقوط ضمان کا مدعی ہے۔ تو بینہ سے ثابت کرنا ضروری ہوگا۔ جیسے اگر وہ یہ کہے کہ مجھے مودع نے غیر کو دینے کی اجازت دے دی تھی تو یہ بات بینہ کے بغیر قبول نہیں ہوتی ۔
Translation: If he guards with other than them (non-family) or deposits with another, he becomes liable. Hands differ in trust. And because a thing does not contain its like. Placing in another's safe is also giving a deposit, except when he hired the place, then it is guarding in his own safe. Unless fire occurs in his house, so he gives to his neighbor, or he is in a boat and fears drowning so he puts it in another boat, because in that state this is the only way of guarding. So the owner will be pleased with him, and his statement is not accepted without evidence because he claims a necessity that removes liability, so it becomes like claiming permission. Then if the owner demands the deposit and he withholds while able to give, he becomes liable because withholding is transgression. And because once the owner demands, he is no longer pleased with his keeping. So he becomes liable by withholding. Commentary: 'His statement: if he guards with other than him' - if the depositary gives the deposit to someone other than his family for safekeeping or deposits with another, he becomes liable because the owner is only pleased with the possession of the bailee, not another's. It may be said that just as his hand is, so is the hand of another. The answer from 'hands differ' is that it is not so, but hands differ in trust; some are trustworthy, some treacherous. 'And because a thing does not contain' his deposit cannot be kept with another because the owner gave it to him, and a thing does not contain its like, but may contain lesser. Like an agent cannot appoint another agent, nor a mudarib give the capital to another for mudarabah, but can give bidā'ah because it is lesser. If one says he did not give the deposit but placed it in another's safe, the answer from 'placing in a safe' is that placing in another's safe is giving the deposit, so liability attaches. 'His statement: unless it occurs' is an exception from the previous rule; liability only applies when done intentionally, not if done under duress. For example, if his house catches fire so he gives to his neighbor, or he is in a boat and out of fear of drowning transfers to another boat, he is not liable because in that state this is the only way, so the owner would certainly consent. Then if the depositary claims he did so due to the mentioned necessity, his statement is not accepted without evidence because he gave possession to another, which grounds liability, and he claims removal of liability, so he must prove with evidence. Similarly, if he claims the owner permitted him to give to another, it is not accepted without evidence.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf ul-Hidaya explains the ruling when a depositary (mudi') entrusts the deposit to a third party. The base ruling: if the depositary gives the deposit to someone other than his own family (household) or leaves it with another person, he becomes liable (dāmin). The reason is that the owner only consented to the possession of the bailee, not a stranger. The passage then addresses two potential counter-arguments: (1) that another's hand is like his own? The answer: hands differ in trust; some are reliable, others treacherous. (2) That placing in another's safe is not giving the deposit? The answer: placing in another's safe is legally the same as giving the deposit, so liability follows. An exception is then made for cases of necessity: if the depositary acts out of genuine need (e.g., fire in house, fear of drowning in boat), he is not liable because the owner would undoubtedly consent. However, if the depositary claims such necessity, his word is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah), because he is claiming something that removes liability. Similarly, if he claims the owner gave permission to hand over to someone else, that too requires evidence. The passage also covers the scenario where the owner demands the deposit back and the depositary refuses to return it while able to do so: he becomes liable due to transgression (ta'addī), because the owner's consent to his possession ends with the demand. This ruling with proof demonstrates the Hanafi emphasis on the owner's consent and the principle that the depositary acts at his own risk when deviating from the owner's instructions.

Key Terms
ودیعة (wadi'ah): Deposit placed with someone for safekeeping.
مستودع (mustawda'): The depositary (bailee) who receives the deposit.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation, here meaning the depositary becomes liable to pay the value of the deposit if lost due to his transgression.
حرز (hirz): A safe place or container where the deposit is kept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the rational proofs given for the depositary's liability when entrusting to a non-family member?
A) Hands differ in trustworthiness.
B) A thing does not contain its like.
C) Placing in another's safe is giving the deposit.
D) The owner's consent is implied for any person.

Key Takeaway
A depositary becomes liable for entrusting the deposit to a non-family member because the owner's trust is personal, and rational proofs confirm that hands differ, a thing does not imply its like, and placing in another's safe is equivalent to handing over.

intermediate
10 min
Necessity Exceptions and Liability for Refusal to Return the Deposit
Expansion probe on exceptions and refusal liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a depositary is excused from liability for entrusting to a non-family member due to necessity, explain the burden of proof, and state the rule that refusal to return after demand creates liability.

Core Idea
Necessity exempts from liability, but the claimant must prove it; refusing to return after demand is a transgression making the depositary liable.

The passage continues with important exceptions and clarifications. Even when the depositary normally would be liable for handing over to a stranger, genuine emergencies such as fire or fear of drowning remove liability. However, the depositary must prove the necessity if disputed. Additionally, once the owner demands the deposit back, the depositary's refusal to return, while able, is a transgression that triggers liability.

Guided Path
1
Understand the necessity exception
The passage provides two examples: fire in the house (handing to neighbor) and fear of drowning in a boat (transferring to another boat). In such genuine emergencies, the depositary is not liable because the owner would certainly consent.
The exception only applies when the depositary acts under duress (idṭirār), not voluntarily.
2
Burden of proof for claiming necessity
If the depositary claims he acted under necessity, his word is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah). The reason is that he is asserting a cause that removes liability; similar to claiming the owner's permission.
This protects the owner's rights.
3
The demand-refusal rule
If the owner demands the deposit back and the depositary withholds it while being able to return, he becomes liable. The owner's demand ends his consent to the bailee's possession, so retention becomes a transgression.
Liability arises from the moment of refusal, not from the original receipt.

Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf ul-Hidaya explains the ruling when a depositary (mudi') entrusts the deposit to a third party. The base ruling: if the depositary gives the deposit to someone other than his own family (household) or leaves it with another person, he becomes liable (dāmin). The reason is that the owner only consented to the possession of the bailee, not a stranger. The passage then addresses two potential counter-arguments: (1) that another's hand is like his own? The answer: hands differ in trust; some are reliable, others treacherous. (2) That placing in another's safe is not giving the deposit? The answer: placing in another's safe is legally the same as giving the deposit, so liability follows. An exception is then made for cases of necessity: if the depositary acts out of genuine need (e.g., fire in house, fear of drowning in boat), he is not liable because the owner would undoubtedly consent. However, if the depositary claims such necessity, his word is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah), because he is claiming something that removes liability. Similarly, if he claims the owner gave permission to hand over to someone else, that too requires evidence. The passage also covers the scenario where the owner demands the deposit back and the depositary refuses to return it while able to do so: he becomes liable due to transgression (ta'addī), because the owner's consent to his possession ends with the demand. This ruling with proof demonstrates the Hanafi emphasis on the owner's consent and the principle that the depositary acts at his own risk when deviating from the owner's instructions.

Key Terms
اضطرار (idṭirār): Compulsion or dire necessity that forces the depositary to transfer the deposit.
بینہ (bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically two witnesses or a confession, required to prove a claim that removes liability.
متعدّي (muta'addī): One who transgresses, i.e., acts beyond the scope of permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the depositary's word not accepted without evidence when he claims necessity (e.g., fire) for giving the deposit to a non-family member?
A) Because the depositary is generally untrustworthy.
B) Because he is claiming a cause that removes liability, similar to claiming the owner's permission.
C) Because the owner is always present to confirm.
D) Because fires are common and easily proved.

Key Takeaway
The depositary is not liable for entrusting the deposit to a non-family member only if he acted under genuine necessity and can prove it; once the owner demands the deposit, refusal to return creates liability.

The Four Scenarios of Mixing a Deposit and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Classification of mixing types and detailed legal arguments between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of mixing a deposit, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on same-genre mixing, and apply the ruling to determine the depositary's liability or choice.

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit can lead to liability or a choice depends on the type of mixing and the Hanafi imam's view.

This lesson examines the detailed rules when a depositary (mudiʿ) mixes the deposit with his own property. The text from Ashraf al-Hidayah outlines four possible scenarios based on the nature of mixing. The fourth scenario—mixing same genus—is the locus of a classical intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), with practical implications for the depositor's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
From the text, list the four types of mixing: (1) juxtaposition with easy differentiation, (2) juxtaposition with difficult differentiation, (3) mixing different genera by commingling, (4) mixing same genus with same genus.
Scenarios 1-3 have consensus rulings; scenario 4 is disputed.
2
Determine liability for each scenario
For each scenario, state whether the depositary is liable (dāmin) by consensus or subject to ikhtilāf. Note that scenarios 2 and 3 incur liability, scenario 1 does not, and scenario 4 is disputed.
Consensus (ijmāʿ) exists for the first three scenarios.
3
State the intra-Hanafi positions on scenario 4
Abu Hanifa holds that mixing same genus makes the depositary automatically liable. The Sahibayn give the depositary a choice: either pay compensation (daman) or become a partner (sharīk) in the mixture.
Include the cross-madhhab views: Shafiʿi and Hanbali agree with Abu Hanifa; Maliki makes partnership compulsory.
4
Analyze the rational proofs (istidlāl)
The Sahibayn argue that since the owner cannot physically retrieve his exact property but can get its value through division, the mixing is partial destruction (istihlāk min wajh). Abu Hanifa argues it is total destruction (istihlāk min kull wajh) because division is a consequence of partnership, not a cause for it.
The distinction hinges on whether the possibility of division preserves the owner's right in meaning.
5
Apply the ruling: effect of ibrāʾ (release)
If the depositor releases the depositary from liability, according to Abu Hanifa the depositor has no claim to the mixture (his right was a debt, now extinguished). According to the Sahibayn, the option of compensation falls and partnership becomes fixed.
This shows the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in a concrete scenario.

الرَّضَاعِ وَنَظِيرُهُ خَلْطُ الدَّرَاهِمِ بِمِثْلِهَا إِذَابَةً لِأَنَّهُ يَصِيرُ مَائِعًا بِالْإِذَابَةِ ترجمه صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس کو اپنا عین حق ملنا صورۃ ممکن نہیں اور معنی ممکن ہے اس کے رسائی متعذر ہے اور بٹوارہ کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں۔ کیونکہ یہ شرکت کے احکام میں سے ہے تو موجب شرکت ہونے کے لائق نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر مودع نے خلط کو بری کر دیا۔ تو اس کے لئے مخلوط پر کوئی راہ نہیں۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک کیونکہ اس کا حق صرف دین میں تھا جو ساقط ہو چکا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک بری کرنے سے صرف تاوان کا اختیار ساقط ہوا تو مخلوط میں شرکت متعین ہو گئی ۔ تل کے تیل کو روغن زیتون کے
Translation: If the depositary mixed the deposit with his own property in such a way that it cannot be distinguished, he becomes liable, then the depositor has no way to retrieve his specific property. Regarding the mixing of the deposit, according to Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn say that if he mixes it with the same genus, he may become a partner if he wishes. For example, mixing white dirhams with white dirhams, black dirhams with black dirhams, or wheat with wheat, barley with barley. (Then page 198: Issue of mixing the deposit). 'His statement: And if he mixes it...' If the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property, there are four scenarios: 1) Mixing through juxtaposition with easy differentiation, e.g., white dirhams with black dirhams, dirhams with dinars, walnuts with almonds: by consensus, he is not liable. 2) Mixing through juxtaposition with difficult differentiation, e.g., wheat with barley: by consensus, he is liable. 3) Mixing different genera through commingling, e.g., sesame oil with olive oil: by consensus, he is liable. 4) Mixing same genus with same genus, e.g., white dirhams with white dirhams, black dirhams with black dirhams, wheat with wheat, barley with barley: this case is disputed. According to Abu Hanifa, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad, he is liable. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he has a choice: either pay compensation or become a partner in the mixture. (Malik said: he becomes a partner without choice.) The proof of the Sahibayn: he cannot reach his specific right in form (physically), but can reach it in meaning (by value) through division, so it is destruction from one perspective but not from another, so he may incline to whichever he wishes. The proof of Abu Hanifa: it is destruction from all perspectives because it is an act that makes reaching his specific right impossible, and division is not considered because division is a consequence of partnership, not a cause for it. If the depositor releases the mixer (ibrā'), according to Abu Hanifa he has no path to the mixture because his right was only a debt which has fallen. According to them (Sahibayn), with release the option of compensation falls and partnership in the mixture becomes fixed. Mixing oil (sesame) with olive oil and every liquid with a different genus obligates the owner's right to become compensation, by consensus, because it is destruction in form and meaning due to impossibility of division considering different genera. Similarly, mixing wheat with barley in the correct view, because one does not lack grains of the other, so differentiation and division become impossible. If he mixes a liquid with its own genus (same liquid), then according to Abu Hanifa the owner's right becomes compensation; according to Abu Yusuf, the lesser becomes a follower of the greater, considering the majority of parts; according to Muhammad, partnership in any case, because the genus does not overpower the genus in his view, as previously in the chapter on radā'. Similarly, mixing dirhams with their like by melting, because it becomes a liquid by melting. Urdu translation of the arguments.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf ul-Hidayah deals with the ruling when a depositary (mudi') mixes the deposited property with his own wealth. It first outlines four scenarios of mixing: (1) Mixing by juxtaposition where the deposited item can still be distinguished (e.g., white coins with black coins): no liability by consensus. (2) Mixing by juxtaposition where differentiation is difficult (e.g., wheat with barley): liability by consensus. (3) Mixing different genera by commingling (e.g., sesame oil with olive oil): liability by consensus. (4) Mixing same genus with same genus (e.g., same type of coins or grain): this is the disputed case. Abu Hanifa, along with al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, holds that the depositary becomes liable (dāmin) because the mixing makes it impossible to return the exact object. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) give the depositary a choice: either pay compensation or become a partner in the mixture. Their reasoning: the owner cannot physically get his exact property, but he can get its value through division (since the mixture is homogeneous), so it is partial destruction, and the depositary may choose. Abu Hanifa argues it is total destruction because division is a remedy of partnership, not a cause for it, and the owner's right is only to his specific property. The passage then discusses the effect of release (ibrā'): if the owner releases the depositary from liability, Abu Hanifa says he has no claim to the mixture (his right was a debt, now extinguished), while the Sahibayn say the choice of compensation falls away and partnership becomes fixed. Further sub-issues: mixing different liquids is always destructive by consensus; mixing wheat with barley is similar due to the impossibility of perfect separation; mixing same-genre liquids leads to further ikhtilaf among the three founders. This passage is a classic example of intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) and shows the nuanced application of the principle of istihlāk (destruction).

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposited property with the depositary's own property.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Destruction or consumption of the property such that it cannot be returned specifically.
ممازجة (mumāzajah): Commingling where the substances merge, e.g., liquids mixed together.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership in the mixture; the depositor becomes a co-owner proportionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what options does a depositary have when he mixes the deposit with identical property of the same genus?
A) He must pay compensation only
B) He can either pay compensation or become a partner in the mixture
C) He becomes a partner automatically
D) He is not liable at all

Key Takeaway
The classification of mixing types determines liability; for same-genre mixing, Abu Hanifa holds automatic liability while the Sahibayn give a choice, with the mufta bihi being Abu Hanifa's view in most fatwa collections.

Ruling on Mixing a Deposit with the Depositary's Property
Explains four scenarios of mixing a deposit and the dispute on same-genre mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios of mixing a deposit, state the ruling for each, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on same-genre mixing with its proofs and practical consequence.

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit can result in liability or partnership depending on distinguishability and genre; same-genre mixing is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This lesson examines the detailed rules for mixing a deposit (wadi'ah) with the depositary's own property. The text from Al-Hidayah presents four scenarios and then focuses on the disputed case of mixing same-genre items, where Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions differ. You will learn the proofs and the practical consequence of discharging the depositary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four mixing scenarios
The text lists: 1) adjacency with easy distinction (no liability), 2) adjacency with difficult distinction (liability), 3) intermingling of different kinds (liability), 4) mixing of same kind (disputed). List each and its ruling.
2
Understand the basis for the non-disputed rulings
For scenarios 1-3, the ruling is by consensus (bila khilaf). The key factor is the possibility of retrieval: if the deposit can be retrieved in form (easy distinction) then no liability; if not, liability.
3
Focus on the disputed fourth scenario
For same-genre mixing (e.g., white dirhams with white dirhams), there is ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa (liability) and the two companions (choice of liability or partnership). Understand the two proofs.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa: mixing is complete consumption (istihlak) because exact retrieval is impossible; division (qismah) is an effect of partnership, not a cause. The two companions: consumption is only in form, not in meaning, because division by weight/measure is possible, so partial consumption gives choice.
5
Explore the consequence of discharge (ibra')
If the depositor discharges the depositary: according to Abu Hanifa, the depositor loses all rights (only debt remained); according to the two companions, the choice of liability falls, so partnership is fixed.
6
Examine the special case of liquids
For same-genre liquids, further intra-Hanafi differences: Abu Yusuf says lesser follows greater; Muhammad says partnership always; Abu Hanifa says liability. Also melting dirhams analogy.
