Deposit with Two Persons: Divisible vs Indivisible Property
Explains ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on deposit with two persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings for divisible and indivisible deposits when deposited with two persons according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and explain the opposing view of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
With two depositaries, the owner's consent to both limits each to his share; only indivisible property permits one to keep the whole with permission.

When a depositor entrusts an item to two persons, a question arises: may one of them hand over the entire deposit to the other? Imam Abu Hanifa gives a nuanced answer based on whether the property is divisible or not, while his two disciples (Sahibayn) hold a simpler view that each may transfer to the other with permission. This intra-madhhab dispute rests on how broadly the owner's consent is interpreted. In this lesson, we examine the ruling, the arguments, and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and ruling
The passage begins by stating the ruling for a deposit with two persons. First, note the distinction between divisible and indivisible property. According to Abu Hanifa, if the property is divisible, the depositaries must divide it and each preserve his half; neither may give the whole to the other without permission. If indivisible, one may keep the whole with the other's permission.
Divisible property includes items like grain, money, or anything that can be split into parts without losing value.
2
Recognize the extension to other contracts
The ruling is also applied to two pawns (e.g., a pawned item held by two pawn-holders) and two agents authorized to purchase. The same distinction holds: if the object is divisible, they cannot transfer the whole; if indivisible, they may with permission.
This shows a consistent principle in Hanafi law regarding joint custody.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that in both cases, each depositary may hand over to the other with the latter's permission, and the giver is not liable if the item is lost while with the receiver. Their reasoning: the owner was satisfied with their trustworthiness, so each is authorized to transfer to the other.
This is a more permissive view based on general authorization.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the owner's consent is to both preserving together, not to one preserving alone. Since the act of preservation, when applied to something divisible, covers only a part, handing over the whole exceeds the owner's consent. Therefore the giver commits a transgression (ta'addi) and is liable for the entire item. The receiver is not liable because, according to Abu Hanifa, the second depositary (mudi' al-muwadda') is not liable without transgression.
The key point: consent is limited to the scope of the deposit contract.
5
Understand the exception for indivisible property
For indivisible items, continuous joint custody is impossible, so alternation (muhāyāh) is necessary. The owner impliedly consents to one holding the whole from time to time. Therefore, the giver is not liable when handing over with the other's permission.
Impossibility of joint preservation creates an implied consent to alternation.
6
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: if a divisible deposit is handed over entirely to one depositary and then lost, under Abu Hanifa the giver is liable for the full value; under the Sahibayn, neither is liable (the owner bears the loss). This affects the risk each depositary takes.
This is important for fatwa practice; later Hanafi preference often leans towards the Sahibayn's view in many cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ أَوَدَعَ رَجُلٌ عِندَ رَجُلَيْنِ شَيْئًا مِمَّا يُقْسَمُ لَمْ يَجُزْ أَنْ يَدْفَعَهُ أَحَدَهُمَا إِلَى الْآخَرِ وَلَكِنَّهُمَا يَقْتَسِمَانِهِ فيحفظ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا نِصْفَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ مِمَّا لَا يُقْسَمُ جَازَ أَنْ يَحْفَظَهُ اَحَدُهُمَا بِإِذْنِ الْآخَرِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ وَكَذَالِكَ الْجَوَابُ عِنْدَهُ فِي الْمُرْتَهِنَيْنِ وَالْوَكِيْلَيْنِ بِالشّرَاءِ إِذَا سَلَّمَ اَحَدُهُمَا إِلَى الْآخَرِ وَقَالَا لَأَحَدِهِمَا أَنْ يَحْفَظَ بِإِذْنِ الْآخَرِ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ لَهُمَا أَنَّهُ رَضِيَ بِاِمَانَتِهِمَا فَكَانَ لِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِّنْهُمَا أَنْ يُسَلَّمَ إِلَى الْآخَرِ وَلَا يَضْمَنُهُ كَمَا فِي مَا لَا يُقْسَمُ وَلَهُ إِنَّهُ رَضِيَ بِحِفْظِهِمَا وَلَمْ يَرْضَ بِحِفْظِ أَحَدِهِمَا كُلَّهُ لَانَّ الْفِعْلَ متَى أُضِيْفَ إِلَى مَا يَقْبَلُ الْوَصْفَ بِالتَّجَزَى يَتَنَاوَلُ الْبَعْضَ دُونَ الْكُلِ فَوَقَعَ التَّسْلِيمُ إِلَى الْآخَرِ مِنْ غَيْرِ رِضَاءِ الْمَالِكِ فَيَضْمَنُ الدَّافِعُ وَلَا يَضْمَنُ الْقَابِضُ لِاَنَّ مُودَعَ الْمُؤدَعُ عِندَهُ لا يَضْمَنَ وَهَذَا بِخِلَافِ مَا لَا يُقْسَمُ لأنَّهُ لَمَّا أَوْدَعُهُمَا وَلَا يُمْكِنُهُمَا الْإِجْتِمَاعُ عَلَيْهِ آنَاءَ اللَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ وَامْكَنَهُمَا الْمُهَايَاةُ كَانَ الْمَالِكُ رَاضِبًا بدفع الكُلِّ إِلَى أَحَدِهِمَا فِي بَعْضَ الْأَحْوَالِ
He said: If a man deposits with two men something that is divisible, it is not permissible for one of them to hand it over to the other, but they should divide it and each preserve his half. If it is something indivisible, it is permissible for one of them to preserve it with the permission of the other, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly, the ruling with him applies to two pawns and two agents for purchase when one hands over to the other. And they (the two, i.e., Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: For each of them it is permissible to preserve with the permission of the other in both cases because he (the owner) was satisfied with their trustworthiness, so each of them may hand over to the other and will not be liable, as in the case of the indivisible. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that he was satisfied with the preservation of both, not with the preservation of one alone entirely, because the act, when attributed to something that admits division, covers a part not the whole. So the delivery to the other was without the owner's consent, so the giver is liable, and the receiver is not liable because the person with whom the deposit is deposited (second depositary) is not liable according to him. And this is unlike the indivisible because when he deposited with them and it is not possible for them both to be together at all times of night and day, and it is possible for them to alternate, the owner is satisfied with the handing over of the whole to one of them in some situations.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a deposit entrusted to two persons. The base ruling distinguishes between divisible and indivisible property. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the deposited item is divisible (like measured or weighed goods), it is not permissible for one of the two depositaries to hand over the entire item to the other. Instead, they must divide it and each preserve his half. If the item is indivisible (like a slave or a garment), then one may keep it with the permission of the other. The same applies to pawns and purchasing agents. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that in both cases, each depositary may hand over the deposit to the other with the latter's permission, and the giver is not liable. Their reasoning is that the owner consented to their trustworthiness, so each is authorized to transfer to the other. Abu Hanifa responds that the owner consented to both of them preserving it, not to one preserving it entirely. When the act of preservation is attributed to something that can be divided, it implies only a part, not the whole. Therefore, the delivery of the entire deposit by one to the other is without the owner's consent, making the giver liable. The receiver is not liable because Abu Hanifa holds that the second depositary is not liable (a principle: 'al-mudi' al-muwadda' indahu la yadman'). The exception for indivisible items is based on necessity — since continuous joint preservation is impossible, the owner impliedly consents to one keeping it at times.

Key Terms
مُودَع (mudi'): The person with whom something is deposited; the depositary.
مُودَع المُودَع (mudi' al-muwadda'): A second depositary to whom the first depositary entrusts the deposit.
مَُهايَاة (muhāyāh): Alternation or division of time between co-depositaries for preservation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a depositor entrusts a divisible item to two persons, and one of them hands over the entire item to the other without permission, what is the ruling?
A) The giver is liable for the whole item.
B) Neither is liable because the owner consented to both.
C) The receiver is liable as the new depositary.
D) The giver is liable only if the item is lost.

Key Takeaway
The scope of consent in joint deposits hinges on property divisibility: divisible property limits each to his share, while indivisible property permits alternation without liability.

Ruling on Violating Owner's Prohibition Regarding Sub-Deposit
Ruling statement on liability when depositary violates owner's prohibition regarding sub-delivery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a depositary delivers a deposit to his wife despite the owner's explicit prohibition, and explain why he is not liable.

Core Idea
Not every prohibition by the owner imposes liability — only conditions essential for safekeeping are binding.

In daily life, a person may entrust something to another and add personal restrictions. But are all such restrictions legally binding? This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies that when the owner says 'do not give it to your wife,' the depositary is still free to do so without incurring liability, because the prohibition is seen as advice, not a condition. Understanding this nuance prevents misapplication of trust law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the prohibition
The owner prohibits delivery to the depositary's wife. The depositary delivers anyway. Identify the act: depositary handing over the deposit.
2
Determine the ruling
The text rules: لا يضمن (no liability). The depositary is not liable for loss or damage.
3
Understand the legal reason
The prohibition is considered nasihah (advice), not a binding condition. The wife is trustworthy in household matters, so delivery is not ta'addi (transgression).
4
Apply to similar cases
The same principle applies to indispensable employees: if owner forbids giving to any employee, but depositary gives to a necessary servant, no liability.
5
Note the ruling's limits
If the prohibition had been essential for safekeeping (e.g., 'don't give to anyone'), liability might arise. This ruling is about personal, non-essential restrictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قَالَ صَاحِبُ الْوَدِيْعَةِ الْمُوْدَعَ لَا تُسَلَّمُهَا إِلَى زَوْجَتِكَ فَسَلَّمَهَا إِلَيْهَا لَا يَضْمَنُ وَفِي الْجَامِعِ الصَّغِيْرِ إِذَا نَهَاهُ أَنْ يَدْفَعَهَا إِلَى أَحَدٍ مِنْ عَمَالِهِ فَدَفَعَهَا إِلَى مَنْ لَا بُدَّ مِنْهُ لَا يَضْمَنُ كَمَا إِذَا كَانَتِ الْوَدِيْعَةُ دَابَّةٌ فَنَهَاهُ عَن
And if the owner of the deposit said to the depositary: 'Do not hand it over to your wife,' and he handed it over to her, he is not liable. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If he forbade him from giving it to any of his employees, and he gave it to someone who is indispensable, he is not liable, as if the deposit is an animal and he forbade him from...
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This is a ruling statement: when the owner of a deposit explicitly prohibits the depositary from giving the deposit to his (the depositary's) wife, but the depositary nevertheless gives it to her, he is not held liable if it is lost or damaged thereafter. The reason is that the prohibition is considered a recommendation (nasihah) rather than a binding legal condition. The depositary's wife is a reliable person within his household, and entrusting household affairs often involves her. Therefore, the depositary's act is not a transgression (ta'addi) voiding the trust. The text also references a ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir: if the owner prohibits giving the deposit to any of the depositary's employees, but the depositary gives it to someone indispensable (like a necessary servant or helper), again he is not liable. An example is given of a prohibition concerning an animal's handler, but the text cuts off. This ruling highlights that restrictions imposed by the owner on the depositary's choice of sub-depositaries are not generally binding to the extent of imposing liability unless they are essential for safekeeping.

Key Terms
لَا يَضْمَنُ (lā yaḍman): He is not liable, i.e., does not incur responsibility for compensation if the deposit is lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Hidayah, if the owner of a deposit tells the depositary, 'Do not give it to your wife,' and the depositary gives it to her and it is lost, what is the ruling?
A) The depositary is liable because he disobeyed.
B) The depositary is not liable because the wife is trustworthy.
C) The depositary is liable only if he gave it without her permission.
D) The owner must prove negligence.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a depositary does not become liable merely by disobeying the owner's preference; liability requires a true breach of trust that jeopardizes the deposit's safety.

Deposit with Two Persons: Divisible vs Indivisible Property
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when one of two depositaries hands over a divisible deposit to the co-depositary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding handing over a divisible deposit to a co-depositary and identify the practical difference in liability.

Core Idea
When a divisible deposit is entrusted to two persons, each can only guard his share – handing over the whole creates liability for the giver per Abu Hanifa.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of a depositary's authority when two people are entrusted with a single deposit. The key factor is whether the property can be physically divided. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi jurists interpret the owner's implied consent and the resulting liability for loss.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the scenario and the disputed act
Clearly describe the factual scenario: a depositor entrusts a divisible item (e.g., wheat) to two depositaries. One depositary then hands the entire item to the co-depositary without the owner's explicit permission. The dispute is whether this action is valid and whether liability arises.
Divisible property is one that can be physically divided without affecting its value or utility, such as grains, liquids, money.
2
Trace the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and his talil
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that handing over the divisible deposit to the co-depositary is not permissible. If done, the giver becomes liable (damin) for the share he handed over (i.e., his half), but the receiver is not liable. The rationale: the owner's act of entrusting (istihfaz) when connected to a divisible object applies to each part separately (al-fi'l yusha'u al-juz' la al-kull). Therefore, the owner only consented to each depositary guarding his own part; handing over the whole exceeds consent.
This is a form of talil – reasoning based on the attribute of divisibility.
3
Trace the ruling of the Sahibayn and their talil
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that each depositary may hand over the entire deposit to the other with the latter's permission, and neither becomes liable. Their proof: the owner is satisfied with the trustworthiness of both, so each has the implied authority to delegate custody. This is consistent with the rule for indivisible property, where such delegation is universally allowed.
The Sahibayn do not distinguish between divisible and indivisible; they focus on the owner's general satisfaction.
4
Compare the two positions and identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference (thamarah) is liability: if the deposit is lost while in the hands of the co-depositary, according to Abu Hanifa the depositary who handed it over must compensate the owner for his share; according to the Sahibayn, neither pays. Also note that for indivisible property, both sides agree that delegation is permissible because the owner necessarily consents to alternate custody.
The dispute extends to analogous parties: two mortgagees, two purchasing agents, executors, and trustees.

اشرف الہدایہ جلد- یاز دہم ... ۲۰۵
كتاب الوديعة
کی اجازت سے حفاظت کر سکتا ہے اور یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور یونہی حکم ہے ان کے نزدیک دومرتبہنوں اور خرید کے کی [؟] اور وصیین اور عدلین وکلا خرید میں سے ایک دوسرے کو سپرد کر دے۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ ہر ایک کو اختیار ہے کہ دوسرے کی اجازت سے حفاظت کرے دونوں صورتوں میں۔ ان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مودع ان دونوں کی امانت سے راضی ہے تو ہر ایک کو اختیار ہو گا کہ دوسرے کے سپرد کر دے اور ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ جیسے غیر قابل قسمت میں ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ دونوں کی حفاظت سے راضی ہوا ہے اس سے راضی نہیں ہوا کہ کوئی ایک پوری ودیعت کی حفاظت کرے اس لئے کہ فعل جب ایسی چیز کی طرف مضاف ہو جو ٹکڑے ہونے کی صفت قبول کرتی ہو تو وہ جز کو شامل ہوتا ہے نہ کہ کل کو۔ پس دوسرے کو سپرد کرنا مالک کی رضاء کے بغیر ہوا۔ لہذا دینے والا ضامن ہو گا۔ اور قابض ضامن نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ مودع المودع امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ضامن نہیں ہوتا اور یہ غیر قابل تقسیم کے خلاف ہے اس لئے کہ جب مودع نے دونوں کے پاس ودیعت رکھی ہے۔ جب کہ شب و روز میں ہمہ وقت ان کا مجتمع رہنا ممکن نہیں۔ ہاں یہ ممکن ہے کہ دونوں باری باری حفاظت کریں تو بعض حالات میں کل ودیعت دوسرے کو دینے سے وہ راضی ہے۔ تشریح قوله وان اودع رجل - ایک شخص نے دو آدمیوں کے پاس ایسی چیز ودیعت رکھی جو بنوارہ کے قابل ہے یعنی اس میں تفریق حسی سے کوئی عیب نہیں آتا جیسے مکیلی و موزونی اشیاء تو ان میں سے کسی ایک کے لئے یہ جائز نہیں کہ وہ پوری چیز دوسرے کی حفاظت میں دے دے بلکہ وہ دونوں اس کو تقسیم کر کے نصف نصف کی حفاظت کریں۔ ہاں اگر وہ چیز نا قابل تقسیم ہو جیسے غلام ، سواری ، ثوب واحد طبق وغیرہ تو اس میں یہ جائز ہے کہ ان میں سے کوئی ایک دوسرے کی اجازت ہے اس کی حفاظت کرے۔ یہ امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور دو مرتبہنوں اور خرید کے دو کیلوں کا حکم بھی آپ کے یہاں یہی ہے (وكذا المستبضعين والوصيين والعدلين ) رہن کی مثال جیسے زید نے دو شخصوں کے پاس قابل تقسیم چیز رہن رکھی اور ان میں سے ایک نے وہ چیز دوسرے کو سپرد کر دی تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس حصہ کا ضامن ہوگا۔ اور اگر وہ چیز نا قابل تقسیم ہو تو ایک مرتبین دوسرے کی اجازت سے اس کی حفاظت کر سکتا ہے۔ وکیل خرید کی مثال جیسے زید نے دو شخصوں کو ایک چیز خریدنے کے لئے وکیل کیا اور ان کو ایسا مال شمن دیا جو قابل تقسیم ہے اور ان میں سے ایک وکیل نے وہ کل مال دوسرے وکیل کو دے دیا اور وہ ضائع ہو گیا تو دینے والا نصف کا ضامن ہوگا۔ قوله وقالا لاحدهما صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ ودیعت قابل تقسیم ہو یا نہ ہو۔ بہر دو صورت ان میں سے ہر ایک مودع کو اختیار ہے کہ دوسرے کی اجازت سے حفاظت کرے۔ دلیل ظاہر ہے کہ مودع دونوں کی امانت سے راضی ہوا ہے اس امر پر راضی نہیں ہوا کہ دونوں میں سے ایک امر پر ہوا کہ ہی شخص پوری ودیعت کی حفاظت کرے۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ فعل استحفاظ جب ایسی چیز کی طرف مضاف ہو جو صفت تجربی قبول کرتی ہوتو وہ جز ء کو شامل ہوتا ہے نہ کہ کل کو پس اپنا حصہ دوسرے کو سپرد کرنا مالک کی رضا کے بغیر ہوا۔ لہذا دینے والا ضامن ہو گا۔ البتہ قابض ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک موزع الموزع ضامن نہیں ہوتا۔ بخلاف اس ودیعت کے جو نا قابل تقسیم ہو کہ اس میں ہر ایک کو دوسرے کے سپرد کرنا جائز ہے اس لئے کہ جب مودع نے اس کو دونوں کے پاس ودیعت رکھا ہے اور وہ جانتا ہے کہ شب و روز میں ہمہ وقت ان دونوں کا مجتمع رہنا ناممکن ہے۔ تو یہ اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ وہ ان کے باری باری حفاظت کرنے سے راضی ہے۔
He may guard with permission, and this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and similarly the ruling is for two mortgagees and two buyers [؟] and executors and trustees and two purchasing agents — one may hand over to the other. The Sahibayn say: each has the option to guard with the other's permission in both cases. Their proof: the depositor is satisfied with the trust of both, so each may hand over to the other without liability, as in the indivisible case. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: he was satisfied with the guarding by both, not by one alone guarding the entire deposit, because when an action is attributed to something that admits division, it applies to the part, not the whole. So handing over to the other is without the owner's consent; thus the giver is liable, but the receiver is not liable because, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the depositary's depositary is not liable. This differs from the indivisible case because when the depositor placed the deposit with both knowing they cannot be together at all times, he is satisfied with them guarding alternately, so handing over the whole is permissible in some situations. Commentary on 'And if a man deposits': A man deposits with two persons an item that can be divided (i.e., division does not cause a defect), like measured or weighed items. Then it is not permissible for either to hand over the entire item to the other; rather, they must divide it and each guard half. But if the item is indivisible, like a slave, riding animal, single garment, or vessel, then it is permissible for one to guard it with the other's permission. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and similarly the ruling for two mortgagees, two purchasing agents, executors, and trustees. For example, if Zayd pawns a divisible item with two persons and one hands it to the other, according to Imam Abu Hanifa he is liable for that portion. If indivisible, one mortgagee may guard it with the other's permission. Example for purchasing agents: Zayd appoints two agents to buy an item and gives them money that is divisible; one agent gives all the money to the other and it is lost; the giver is liable for half. The statement 'And they said: each one': The Sahibayn say whether the deposit is divisible or not, in both cases each depositary has the option to guard with the other's permission. The proof is apparent: the depositor is satisfied with the trust of both; he is not satisfied that any single person guards the entire deposit. The reason: when the act of entrusting guarding is attributed to something that admits division, it applies to the part, not the whole. So handing over his portion to the other is without the owner's consent; thus the giver is liable. However, the receiver is not liable because, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the depositary's depositary is not liable. Unlike the indivisible deposit, where it is permissible for each to hand over to the other, because when the depositor placed it with both knowing they cannot be together at all times, this indicates he is satisfied with them guarding alternately.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) regarding a deposit (wadi'ah) placed with two persons. The central question: when the item deposited is divisible, may one of the two depositaries hand over the entire item to the other for safekeeping? Imam Abu Hanifa says no; each must guard only his own share. The Sahibayn say yes; either may hand over to the other with permission, and neither becomes liable. The reasoning turns on the nature of the owner's consent. According to Abu Hanifa, when the owner says 'I entrust you both,' he consents to each guarding half, not one guarding the whole, because the action of entrusting (istihfaz) when attributed to an object that can be divided applies to each part individually. Therefore, if one gives his half to the other without the owner's explicit permission, he acts without consent and becomes liable (damin) for that half. The receiver is not liable because, in Abu Hanifa's view, a sub-depositary (mudi' al-mudi') is not liable. For indivisible property (like a slave or animal), everyone agrees it is permissible for one to guard with the other's permission, because the owner, knowing they cannot always be together, implicitly consents to alternate custody. The Sahibayn argue that the owner's trust in both allows each to delegate to the other in either case. The passage also extends this ruling to analogous parties: two mortgagees (murtahinayn), two purchasing agents (wakilay sharā'), two executors (wasiyyayn), and two trustees ('adlayn). For example, if two mortgagees hold a divisible pawn and one hands it to the other, Abu Hanifa holds the giver liable for his share. Similarly for agents with divisible money. This is a foundational ruling in the law of deposits, illustrating how Hanafi jurists resolve questions of consent and liability through textual analysis (talil) of the owner's intent.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A contract of deposit where one person entrusts property to another for safekeeping without reward.
مودع (mudi' (depositor)): The depositor who entrusts property to another.
مودع (mudi' (depositary)): The depositary who accepts the deposit for safekeeping.
صاحبين (al-Sahibayn): Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two foremost disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa.
ضامن (damin): Liable for compensation (damān) in case of loss or damage.
مرتهن (murtahin): A pledgee (creditor) who holds a pawn (rahn) as security for a debt.
وصي (wasiyy): An executor appointed in a will to manage the estate.
عدل ('adl): A trustee or umpire appointed to hold disputed property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if two persons are entrusted with a divisible deposit and one hands the entire deposit to the other without the owner's permission, who is liable?
A) Only the receiver is liable.
B) Neither is liable because the owner trusted both.
C) The giver is liable for his share, but the receiver is not liable.
D) Both are jointly liable for the entire deposit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school divides on whether a depositary of a divisible item may hand it to a co-depositary; Abu Hanifa restricts this due to the owner's divided consent, while the Sahibayn allow it based on general trust.
